Certainty of Lying in Conditional Divorce Based on Internal States
Critical refinement of Qadi Khan's ruling on conditional divorce and internal states

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting a wife's report of an internal state in a conditional divorce, and identify when certainty of lying overrides her report.

Core Idea
For internal states, the wife's report is accepted unless external evidence gives certainty of her lying.

Conditional divorce often hinges on internal states like pleasure or love, which are inherently subjective. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a case from Qadi Khan where a husband beats his wife and she claims she is pleased. The initial ruling rejects her report because the beating provides external evidence of lying. A later authority refines the principle: as a general rule, internal states are taken from the wife's report, but if there is tangible proof of falsehood, her report is disregarded. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the operative rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Conditional Divorce Scenario
The husband says: 'If I please you, you are divorced.' He then hits her. She claims: 'You pleased me.' The question is whether divorce occurs.
The key fact is the beating — external, observable action.
2
Recognize the Two Conflicting Rulings
Qadi Khan and others rule: No divorce, because we are certain she is lying. Mawlana objects: Pleasure is internal, so her report should be accepted even if we suspect lying, analogizing to the case of love of Hellfire.
The objection relies on a qiyas (analogy) to another internal state case.
3
Understand the Analogy and Its Weakness
The analogy is to a husband who says: 'If you love that Allah punish you with fire, you are divorced.' If she says 'I love', divorce occurs despite any suspicion. The objection claims the pleasure case is the same.
But is the beating irrelevant? The later scholar says no.
4
Distinguish Based on External Evidence of Lying
Al-Nahr explains: The beating causes pain, which is external evidence that she is not pleased. This evidence gives certainty of lying. In the Hellfire case, there is no such external evidence; her internal state is unknowable.
Thus the analogy fails because the two cases differ in the presence of external proof of falsehood.
5
State the Final Operative Ruling
If there is external evidence giving certainty of lying (e.g., beating), her report is rejected and divorce does not occur. Otherwise, her report is accepted and divorce occurs. This is the mufta bihi position.
The principle: certainty of lying overrides the general rule of accepting her report.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر قاضيخان قال لامرأته إن سررتك فأنت طالق فضربها فقالت سرني قالوا: لا تطلق امرأته لأنا نتيقن بكذبها . قال مولانا رضي الله تعالى عنه : وفيه أن السرور مما لا يوقف عليه فينبغي أن يتعلق الطلاق بخبرها ويقبل قولها في ذلك وإن كنا نتيقن بكذبها كما لو قال إن كنت تحبين أن يعذبك الله بنار جهنم فأنت طالق فقالت أحب يقع الطلاق عليها ولو أعطى ألف درهم فقالت لم يسرني كان القول قولها ولا يقع الطلاق لاحتمال أنها طلبت الألفين فلا يسرها الألف اهـ. إشكال وهو قوله : (قلت بينهما (فرق قال في النهر : وقد يفرق بينهما بأن إيلام الضرب القائم بها دليل ظاهر على كذبها بخلاف مجرد محبة العذاب فإنه لا دليل فيه على التيقن بكذبها قوله : (وقوله وإن كنا نتيقن بكذبها ممنوع . أنه لما سمعته عن الهداية من لا يتيقن بكذبها . وبهذا ظهر أنه لو علق بفعل قلبي وأخبرت به فإن تيقنا بكذبها لم يقع وإلا وقع.
Qadi Khan mentioned: A man said to his wife, 'If I please you, you are divorced.' He hit her, and she said, 'You pleased me.' They said: His wife is not divorced because we are certain of her lying. Our master, may Allah be pleased with him, said: In this, the pleasure is something that cannot be known, so divorce should be attached to her report and her statement is accepted in that, even if we are certain of her lying, just as if he said 'If you love that Allah punish you with the fire of Hell, you are divorced' and she said 'I love', divorce occurs upon her. And if he gave her a thousand dirhams and she said 'He did not please me', her word is taken and divorce does not occur, due to the possibility that she asked for two thousand, so a thousand did not please her. End. There is an objection, which is his statement: 'I said there is a difference between them.' He said in al-Nahr: A difference can be made between them because the pain of the beating present in her is apparent evidence of her lying, unlike mere love of punishment, where there is no evidence for certainty of her lying. His statement: 'And even if we are certain of her lying' is prevented. Because what was heard from al-Hidayah is that one is not certain of her lying. And from this it appears that if he conditions on a heart action and she reports it, if we are certain of her lying, it does not occur, otherwise it occurs.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case from Qadi Khan: a husband conditions divorce on pleasing his wife, hits her, and she claims she is pleased. The initial ruling is that she is not divorced because we are certain she is lying (since the hitting obviously caused pain). However, a later authority (Mawlana) objects that pleasure is an internal state and thus the wife's report should be accepted even if we suspect she is lying, analogizing to the case of love of Hellfire where the report is accepted despite possibility. The objection is resolved by noting a difference: the physical pain of beating is an external evidence of her lying, unlike the internal love of punishment. The final ruling is that for internal states, if there is certainty of lying (like here with external evidence), divorce does not occur; otherwise, it does. This refines the principle: the wife's report is accepted unless there is definite proof of falsehood.

Key Terms
التيقن من الكذب (al-tayaqqu min al-kadhib): Certainty that the wife is lying, based on apparent evidence
السرور (al-surur): Pleasure or happiness; an internal state assessed by report

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband hits his wife and she claims she is pleased, what is the ruling on her report?
A) It is accepted because pleasure is internal
B) It is rejected because hitting is external evidence of lying
C) It is accepted only if she takes an oath
D) It is rejected only if the husband accuses her

Key Takeaway
For conditional divorce tied to internal states, the wife's report is accepted unless external evidence provides certainty of her lying, in which case her report is rejected and divorce does not occur.

Conditional Divorce with Multiple Wives or Third-Party States
Rulings when conditional divorce involves two wives or conditions on another person's state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a conditional divorce depends on objective verification, and identify when a wife's or third party's claim is insufficient to trigger the condition.

Core Idea
A wife's self-interested testimony against her co-wife is not accepted; the husband's confirmation is needed for third-party conditions.

When a husband makes divorce conditional on an internal state of another person—such as love, desire, or belief—the Hanafi school requires objective verification. This lesson explores three scenarios from al-Muhit and the transmission of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, showing that a person's claim about another's internal state is not accepted unless the husband confirms it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and the person whose state is relevant
Analyze the husband's statement to determine what condition he sets and whose internal state (love, desire, faith) is involved.
For example, 'the one who loves divorce more' involves the wives themselves; 'if your mother does not desire' involves the mother.
2
Determine if the person is a party to the marriage or a third party
If the person is the wife herself or a co-wife, her claim is self-interested. If it is a third party (mother, someone else), different rules apply.
A co-wife's testimony against her co-wife is inherently biased.
3
Apply the rule for self-interested claims
A wife's claim that she meets the condition (e.g., loves divorce more) is not accepted as evidence against her co-wife because she is a witness in her own favor.
The phrase 'shahidatun 'ala sahibatiha' indicates the unreliability of such testimony.
4
Apply the rule for third-party internal states
For third parties (mother, believer), the husband's confirmation is required. The third party's statement alone is not sufficient because it cannot be verified externally.
Even if the third party is a practicing Muslim, they are not believed over the husband.
5
Conclude that the condition is unfulfilled
Without reliable evidence or the husband's confirmation, the condition remains unverified, so no divorce occurs.
The principle: uncertainty in fulfilling the condition prevents the divorce from taking effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط لو قال لامرأتين أشدكما حباً للطلاق وأشدكما بغضاً له طالق فقالت كل واحدة أنا أشد حباً في ذلك لا يقع شيء لأن كل واحدة مخيرة في حق نفسها شاهدة على صاحبتها بما في ضميرها لأنها تقول أنا أشد حباً منها وهي أقل حباً مني وهي غير مصدقة في الشهادة على صاحبتها فلم يتم الشرط اهـ. وقيد بمحبتها لأنه لو علقه بمحبة غيرها فظاهر ما في المحيط أنه لا بد من تصديق الزوج فإنه قال : لو قال أنت طالق إن لم تكن أمك تهوى ذلك فقالت الأم أنا لا أهوى وكذبها الزوج لا تطلق فإن صدقها طلقت لما عرف. وروى ابن رستم عن محمد أنه لو قال إن كان فلان مؤمناً فأنت طالق لا تطلق لأن هذا لا يعلمه إلا هو ولا يصدق هو على غيره وإن كان هو بين مسلمين يصلي ويحج. ولو قال لآخر لي إليك حاجة فاقضها لي فقال امرأته طالق إن لم أقض حاجتك فقال حاجتي أن تطلق زوجتك فله أن لا يصدقه فيه ولا تطلق زوجته لأنه محتمل للصدق والكذب فلا يصدق على غيره .اهـ.
And in al-Muhit: If he said to his two wives, 'The one of you who is more loving of divorce and more hating of me is divorced', and each said 'I am more loving in that', nothing occurs because each is choosing for herself and testifying against the other regarding what is in her conscience, because she says 'I love more than her' and 'She loves less than me', and she is not believed in testimony against her co-wife, so the condition is not fulfilled. End. And it is restricted to her love because if he conditions on the love of another, the apparent from al-Muhit is that the husband's confirmation is necessary. He said: If he says 'You are divorced if your mother does not desire that' and the mother says 'I do not desire', and the husband denies her, she is not divorced; if he believes her, she is divorced, as known. Ibn Rustom narrated from Muhammad that if he says 'If so-and-so is a believer, you are divorced', she is not divorced because only he knows that, and he is not believed over another. Even if he is among Muslims praying and performing Hajj. And if he said to another, 'I have a need from you, so fulfill it for me', and he said 'My wife is divorced if I do not fulfill your need', then the other said 'My need is that you divorce your wife', he has the right not to believe him in it, and his wife is not divorced, because it is possible truth or falsehood, and he is not believed over another. End.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage covers three scenarios where the condition involves another person. First, if a husband addresses two wives and each claims to be more loving of divorce, no divorce occurs because each wife is a self-interested party and her testimony against the other is not accepted. Second, if the condition involves the wife's mother's desire, the husband's confirmation is needed; he may believe her or not, and if he denies it, the divorce does not occur. Third, if the condition involves a third party's faith or a debt, the third party is not believed over the husband because his internal state is only known to him, and the condition is not fulfilled unless the husband confirms. In all cases, the principle is that one person's statement about another's internal state is not accepted for divorce.

Key Terms
شاهدة على صاحبتها (shahidatun 'ala sahibatiha): Testifying against her co-wife about her internal state

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his two wives, 'The one who loves divorce more is divorced', and each says 'I do', what is the ruling?
A) Both are divorced
B) Neither is divorced
C) The one who first speaks is divorced
D) The husband chooses which to believe

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce, when the condition depends on a person's internal state, their self-interested or sole claim is insufficient; the husband's confirmation or external proof is required.

Ta'liq with Minors: Acceptance of Reports
Rulings on conditional divorce for minor girls and boys regarding menstruation and nocturnal emission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on conditional divorce when the condition is fulfilled by a minor's report of menstruation or nocturnal emission, and explain the basis for the differing narrations regarding a boy.

Core Idea
A minor girl's report of menstruation is accepted; a minor boy's report of nocturnal emission is accepted in the soundest view.

When a husband conditions divorce on a private physical event such as menstruation or nocturnal emission, the fulfillment often depends on the minor's report. This lesson examines how Hanafi fiqh handles these delicate situations, balancing observability and trustworthiness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and the minor's role
The husband suspends divorce (ta'liq) on an event that only the minor can directly know. The question is whether the minor's unsupported statement is accepted to trigger the divorce.
2
Examine the rule for the minor girl (murahiqah)
The text from al-Muhit states that if the girl claims menstruation, she is believed. This is because menstruation is internal and cannot be verified by an external observer.
Her report is accepted without an oath according to al-Wiqayah.
3
Examine the rule for the minor boy (ghulam) and the conflicting narrations
For nocturnal emission, there are two narrations. Hisham's narration says the boy is not believed because the emission is externally observable. Another narration says he is believed, analogizing to the girl's menstruation.
The 'asahh' (most sound) view is that the boy is believed in what benefits and harms him.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The accepted position (mufta bihi) is that the boy is believed, as affirmed by later authorities like al-Muhit. This treats both minors symmetrically unless there is clear external evidence.
The practical difference: under Hisham's narration, the husband could demand physical verification; under the sounder view, the boy's word suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في المرأة فشمل من إذا كانت مراهقة لم تحض بعد لما في المحيط : لو قال لامرأته المراهقة إن حضت فأنت طالق فقالت حضت أو قال لغلامه المراهق إن احتلمت فأنت فقال احتلمت، تصدق المرأة ولا حر يصدق الغلام في رواية هشام لأن الغلام ينظر إليه كيف يخرج منه المني ولا يستطاع ذلك في الحيض لأنها تدخل الدم في الفرج فلا يعلم منها أو من غيرها، وفي رواية يصدق الغلام أيضاً لأن الاحتلام يعرفه غيره كالحيض ولذلك إذا قال احتلمت في الأصح وهي إشكال أمره يصدق فيما له وفيما عليه لأنه أخبر بخبر يحتمل الصدق والكذب فيصدق كالجارية اهـ. ولم أر صريحاً أن المرأة إذا قبل قولها في حقها في الحيض والمحبة فهل يكون بيمينها أو بلا يمين ووقع في الوقاية أنه قال صدقت في حقها خاصة وظاهره أنه لا يمين عليها ويدل عليه قولهم إن الطلاق معلق بإخبارها وقد وجد ولا فائدة في التحليف لأنه وقع حال
And he made it absolute regarding the woman, so it includes a minor girl who has not yet menstruated. Because in al-Muhit: If he said to his minor wife, 'If you menstruate, you are divorced', and she said 'I menstruated', or if he said to his minor boy, 'If you have a nocturnal emission, you are free', and he said 'I had one', the woman is believed, but the boy is not believed in the narration of Hisham because the boy can be seen how semen comes out of him, and that is not possible for menstruation because the blood enters the vagina, so it cannot be known whether it is from her or from something else. In another narration, the boy is also believed because nocturnal emission can be known by others like menstruation. Therefore, if he says 'I had a nocturnal emission' in the most sound view, and it is an issue of his affair, he is believed in what is for and against him because he reports information that may be true or false, so he is believed like a slave girl. End. And I have not seen explicitly whether the woman, when her statement is accepted in her own right regarding menstruation and love, whether it is with her oath or without oath. It occurred in al-Wiqayah that he said: She is believed in her own right only, and its apparent meaning is that there is no oath on her. And it is indicated by their saying that divorce is suspended on her report, and it has occurred, so there is no benefit in swearing her because it has occurred.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
The passage addresses ta'liq with minors. For a minor girl who has not yet menstruated, if the husband conditions divorce on her menstruation and she claims she menstruated, she is believed. For a minor boy (ghulam) if the condition is on his nocturnal emission (ihtilam), there is a difference in narrations: one says he is not believed because his emission is observable externally, whereas menstruation is internal and not easily observable. Another narration says he is believed because nocturnal emission can be known by others similar to menstruation. The text also notes that the wife's report about her own state (menstruation or love) is accepted without an oath, as the divorce is already effective upon her report.

Key Terms
مراهقة (murahiqah): A girl approaching puberty, not yet menstruated
احتلام (ihtilam): Nocturnal emission (wet dream), a sign of puberty for boys

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration of Hisham, if a minor boy says he has had a nocturnal emission in a ta'liq condition, is he believed?
A) Yes, because he knows his own state
B) No, because emission can be observed externally
C) Yes, but only if the husband confirms
D) No, because minors are never believed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a minor girl's report of menstruation is accepted without oath, and the soundest view accepts a minor boy's report of nocturnal emission as well.

Third-Party Conditions in Talaq: The Husband's Confirmation Required
Summarizing the ruling on third-party conditions in talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce when the condition depends on a third party and identify when the husband's confirmation is necessary.

Core Idea
In conditional talaq tied to a third party, the third party's word alone does not bind the husband.

This lesson addresses a critical nuance in conditional divorce: when the husband links the divorce to the action or state of someone else, such as the wife's mother. The question is: can the mother's statement about her own desire trigger the divorce? Al-Ramli summarises the Hanafi position: the husband is not bound by such statements; his confirmation is required.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
The husband says 'You are divorced if your mother does not desire that.' The condition depends on the mother's state of desire.
2
Identify the third party's role
The mother is a third party (ghayr) whose action or state is the subject of the condition.
Third party means anyone other than husband or wife.
3
Apply the rule from the branches
Al-Ramli states that from the furu' it is known: when a condition is tied to a third party's action, that third party is not believed about its fulfillment.
This applies whether the matter is known only to them or not.
4
Determine the outcome
Therefore, the mother's claim that she does not desire is not binding. The divorce does not take effect until the husband confirms the condition.
The husband's word is taken if he denies.
5
Note the basis
The ruling is derived from the broader Hanafi principles governing conditional divorce and the authority of the husband.
Al-Ramli's summary is authoritative (fatawa mutakhkhirin).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لو قال أنت طالق إن لم تكن أمك تهوى ذلك الخ قال الرملي: فقد علم من الفروع أنه إن علق بفعل الغير لا يصدق ذلك الغير عليه سواء كان مما لا يعلم إلا منه أم لا، ولا
His saying: (If he said 'You are divorced if your mother does not desire that' etc.), al-Ramli said: It is known from the branches that if he conditions on the action of another, that other is not believed about it, whether it is something that is only known by him or not, and not...
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This is a concluding remark from al-Ramli that summarizes the principle: in all cases where the condition depends on a third party's action or state, that third party is not believed regarding the fulfillment of the condition, whether the matter is known only to that third party or not. The husband's confirmation is required. This reinforces the earlier rulings about the mother and the third party's faith.

Key Terms
فعل الغير (fi'l al-ghayr): Action of a third party, not the husband or wife

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'You are divorced if your mother desires it', and the mother says 'I desire', what is needed for divorce?
A) The mother's statement alone suffices
B) The husband must confirm the mother's statement
C) The wife must also agree
D) The mother must take an oath

Key Takeaway
In conditional talaq tied to a third party, the husband's confirmation is required; the third party's statement alone is not binding.

Acceptance of Wife's Report in Conditional Divorce
Explaining rulings on accepting wife's internal state reports

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on accepting a wife's report of her internal state in conditional divorce and apply it to distinguish between reports about herself and reports about others.

Core Idea
A wife’s report about her own inner state is accepted unless proven false; reports about others require the husband’s confirmation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a nuanced issue in conditional divorce: when the condition depends on an internal feeling like love or hatred, known only to the person themselves. How does the law treat such reports? The Hanafi school balances certainty and practicality, distinguishing between self-reports and third-party statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of condition
Determine whether the condition hinges on an internal state (love, hate) or an external fact. This lesson focuses on internal states known only to the person.
2
State the general rule for self-reports
The wife's report about her own internal state is accepted as true unless the husband is certain she is lying. If there is no certainty, the divorce occurs according to her report.
3
Analyze the exception for proven falsehood
If the husband is certain of her lying, the divorce does not occur. Certainty must be actual, not mere suspicion. Examples from the passage illustrate when certainty might be attained.
4
Distinguish self-report vs. report about another
When the condition involves another person (e.g., mother's love), the wife's report about that person is not accepted automatically; the husband's confirmation or external evidence is needed.
5
Apply to the two‑wife scenario
If each wife claims to be the one who loves divorce more, each is believed about her own love but not about the other's. Since the condition requires a comparison, neither claim is sufficient, so no divorce occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت : بينهما فرق، وقوله وإن كنا نتيقن بكذبها ممنوع . أنه لما سمعته عن الهداية من لا يتيقن بكذبها . وبهذا ظهر أنه لو علق بفعل قلبي وأخبرت به فإن تيقنا بكذبها لم يقع وإلا وقع. وفي البدائع : إن كنت تكرهين الجنة تعلق بإخبارها بالكراهة مع أنها لا تصل إلى حالة تكره الجنة فقد تيقنا بكذبها. وقد يقال : إن لشدة محبتها للحياة الدنيا تكره الجنة لأنها لا تتوصل إليها إلا بالموت وهي تكرهه فلم يتيقن بكذبها . وهل تكفر المرأة بقولها أنا أحب عذاب جهنم وأكره الجنة؟ قلت ظاهر كلامهم هنا عدمه وفي المحيط لو قال لامرأتيه أشدكما حباً للطلاق وأشدكما بغضاً له طالق فقالت كل واحدة أنا أشد حباً في ذلك لا يقع شيء لأن كل واحدة مخيرة في حق نفسها شاهدة على صاحبتها بما في ضميرها لأنها تقول أنا أشد حباً منها وهي أقل حباً مني وهي غير مصدقة في الشهادة على صاحبتها فلم يتم الشرط اهـ. وقيد بمحبتها لأنه لو علقه بمحبة غيرها فظاهر ما في المحيط أنه لا بد من تصديق الزوج فإنه قال : لو قال أنت طالق إن لم تكن أمك تهوى ذلك فقالت الأم أنا لا أهوى وكذبها الزوج لا تطلق فإن صدقها طلقت لما عرف. وروى ابن رستم عن محمد أنه لو قال إن كان فلان مؤمناً فأنت طالق لا تطلق لأن هذا لا يعلمه إلا هو ولا يصدق هو على غيره وإن كان هو بين مسلمين يصلي ويحج. ولو قال لآخر لي إليك حاجة فاقضها لي فقال امرأته طالق إن لم أقض حاجتك فقال حاجتي أن تطلق زوجتك فله أن لا يصدقه فيه ولا تطلق زوجته لأنه محتمل للصدق والكذب فلا يصدق على غيره .اهـ.
I said: There is a difference between them. And his statement 'even if we are certain of her lying' is objectionable. Because when I heard it from al-Hidayah, one is not certain of her lying. And by this it appears that if he conditioned [divorce] on a heart action and she reported it, then if we are certain of her lying it does not occur, otherwise it occurs. In al-Bada'i': If [he said] 'If you hate Paradise,' it is conditioned on her report of hatred, even though she cannot reach a state of hating Paradise, so we are certain of her lying. But it is said: Due to her intense love of this world, she hates Paradise because she only reaches it through death and she hates death, so we are not certain of her lying. And does the wife become an unbeliever by saying 'I love the punishment of Hell and hate Paradise'? I said: The apparent meaning of their words here is no. And in al-Muhit: If he said to his two wives 'The one of you who loves divorce more and hates it more is divorced,' and each said 'I love it more,' nothing occurs because each one is authoritative regarding herself but is testifying against the other regarding what is in her inner self, because she says 'I love it more than her' and 'she loves it less than me,' and she is not believed in testimony against her co-wife, so the condition is not fulfilled. And it is restricted to her love because if he conditioned it on someone else's love, according to what is apparent in al-Muhit, the husband's confirmation is necessary. For he said: If he said 'You are divorced if your mother does not desire that,' and the mother said 'I do not desire' and the husband denied her, she is not divorced. If he affirms her, she is divorced, as is known. And Ibn Rustam narrated from Muhammad that if he said 'If so-and-so is a believer, you are divorced,' she is not divorced because this is known only to Him (Allah), and one is not believed regarding another even if he appears among Muslims praying and performing Hajj. And if he said to another 'I have a need from you, so fulfill it for me,' and he said 'My wife is divorced if I do not fulfill your need,' and the other said 'My need is that you divorce your wife,' he is not required to believe him in that, and his wife is not divorced because it is possible true or false, so one is not believed regarding another.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) where the condition depends on an inner state, such as love or hatred, which is known only to the person themselves. The author discusses the standard of certainty: if the husband is certain that the wife is lying in her report, the divorce does not occur; otherwise, it does. Examples are given: if a husband says 'If you hate Paradise' and the wife reports hatred, the author notes that some argue one can be certain of her lying because no one truly hates Paradise, but others counter that her love of worldly life might make her hate Paradise because it requires death. Thus, certainty is not absolute. Then the case of two wives: if each claims to be the one who loves divorce more, neither is believed against the other because each can only testify about herself. For a third party's inclination (like the mother), the husband's confirmation is required because the mother's report is not binding on him. Finally, narrations from Muhammad al-Shaybani reinforce that one cannot be believed regarding another's faith or intentions; only Allah knows them. The overall principle: the wife's report of her own inner state is accepted unless proven false, but reports of others require the husband's acceptance or external evidence.

Key Terms
تعليق الطلاق (ta'liq al-talaq): The act of making divorce conditional upon the occurrence of a future event or condition.
الإخبار (al-ikhbar): The report or statement made by a person about their own state or about something.
تصديق (tasdiq): Accepting someone's statement as true.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his two wives, 'The one of you who loves divorce more is divorced,' and each wife says 'I love it more,' what is the ruling according to the passage?
A) Both wives are divorced because each claimed it.
B) Neither wife is divorced because each is testifying against the other without evidence.
C) Only the wife who first spoke up is believed.
D) The husband must decide which one is truthful.

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce involving internal states, the wife is trusted about her own state unless proven false, but her testimony about others is not accepted.

Timing of Divorce Conditioned on Menstruation
Explaining timing of conditioned divorce and oath distinction for menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for the timing of divorce conditioned on menstruation and distinguish between the judicial (qada) and conscience (diyanah) approaches to the wife's report.

Core Idea
Divorce conditioned on menstruation occurs after three days of continuous bleeding, retroactively from first sighting — this is clarification, not retroactive creation.

A husband declares 'You are divorced if you menstruate.' His wife sees blood. Is she divorced immediately? The Hanafi school says no, because the blood must be confirmed as menstruation by lasting three days. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning, the principle of tabyin (clarification), and the important distinction between what is accepted in court (qada) and what is true before Allah (diyanah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its effect
Determine what the husband said: 'If you menstruate, you are divorced.' Note that the condition is the occurrence of menstruation, not the mere sighting of blood.
The ruling applies whether the condition uses 'in', 'with', or 'if'.
2
Understand the requirement of continuity (istimrar)
Recognize that in Hanafi fiqh, blood is not legally menstruation until it continues for three consecutive days (with their nights). The sighting alone is speculative.
This is based on the minimum duration of hayd.
3
Grasp the concept of tabyin (clarification)
Once three days elapse, it becomes clear (tabyin) that the blood was menstruation from the beginning. The divorce is effective from the first sighting, not created later. This is not retroactive (istinad) but clarificatory.
Contrast with retroactive rulings where the effect is created back in time.
4
Apply the rule to the slave analogy
The same principle applies to freeing a slave conditioned on menstruation. The slave is not freed until three days pass, but then freedom dates back, affecting tort liability.
If the slave commits a tort during the three days, he is treated as free after clarification.
5
Distinguish between judicial and conscience approaches
In court (qada), the wife's report of menstruation is accepted without oath. In conscience (diyanah), a distinction is made: for love-based conditions, her report binds her; for menstruation, if she lies, the divorce does not take effect before Allah.
This nuance ensures that the law does not force a woman to violate her conscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبرؤية الدم لا يقع فإن استمر ثلاثاً وقع من حين رأت) يعني لا يقع برؤيته فيما إذا علق الطلاق بحيضها، سواء كان بإن» أو بـ«في» أو «مع» نحو أنت طالق في حيضك أو مع حيضك أو إن حضت لأنه لم يتحقق كونه حيضاً حينئذ، فإذا استمر حينئذ ثلاثة أيام بلياليها وقع الطلاق من حين رأت الدم لأنه بالامتداد تبين أنه حيض من الابتداء فيجب على المفتي أن يعينه فيقول طلقت من حين رأت الدم. وليس هذا من باب الاستناد وإنما هو من باب التبيين ولذا قال من حين رأت وقال المصنف في شرح المجمع : إنه تبين بالانتهاء أنه حيض من الابتداء. وأظهر منه ما في المحيط : لو قال لها عبده حر إن حضت فقالت رأيت الدم وصدقها الزوج لا يحكم بعتقه قوله أنت حر قبل موتي بشهر فمات بعده بشهر وقد جنى العبد كان حكمه حكم العبيد عند ا أبي حنيفة لأن ثمة العتق يثبت مستنداً والاستناد لا يظهر في حق الفائت والمتلاشي ، فإن قال الزوج انقطع الدم في الثلاثة وأنكرت المرأة والعبد فالقول لهما لأن الزوج أقر بوجود شرط العتق ظاهراً لأن رؤية الدم في وقته يكون حيضاً ولهذا تؤمر بترك الصلاة والصوم ثم ادعى عارضاً يخرج المرئي من أن يكون حيضاً فلا يصدق فإن صدقته المرأة وكذبه العبد في الأيام الثلاثة فالقول لهما وإن كان بعدها فالقول للعبد اهـ. وفي الكافي بد من تصديق الزوج فيهما أو البينة فيما يثبت بها من الأمر الذي يعلم تأمل. قوله : (وظاهره أنه لا يمين عليها أقره عليه في النهر وهذا في القضاء ظاهر، وأما في الديانة فينبغي التفرقة بين الحيض والمحبة لأن تعلق الطلاق بإخبارها إنما هو في المحبة، أما في الحيض فلا ويدل عليه ما مر من أنها إن كانت كاذبة في الإخبار تطلق في التعليق بالمحبة وفي التعليق بالحيض لا تطلق فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى إلى آخر ما مر فتدبر وفي حواشي مسكين نقل الحموي عن رمز المقدسي أن عليها اليمين بالإجماع إذ ليس هذا من المواضع المستثناة من قولهم كل من قبل قوله فعليه اليمين اهـ. قلت: ولا يخفى ما فيه كيف وقد مر أن الشرع جعلها أمينة فيما تخبر به عن الحيض والطهر وأن المنظور إليه شرعاً في حقها الإخبار به وكذا ما يأتي من أنها لو اخبرت ثم رجعت لا يرتفع الطلاق فإن هذا كالصريح فيما ذكره المؤلف ذكره المؤلف، نعم يقيد في الحيض بالقضاء لا الديانة لما علمت تأمل .
His statement: (And by the sight of blood it does not occur, but if it continues for three [days] it occurs from the time she saw it) means it does not occur by sighting it when divorce is conditioned on her menstruation, whether with 'in' or 'in' or 'with', such as 'You are divorced in your menstruation' or 'with your menstruation' or 'if you menstruate', because it is not confirmed that it is menstruation at that time. So if it then continues for three days with their nights, the divorce occurs from the time she saw the blood, because by extension it becomes clear that it was menstruation from the beginning. So the mufti must specify and say: 'You are divorced from the time she saw the blood.' This is not a matter of retroactivity but rather of clarification; hence he said 'from when she saw it.' And the author said in Sharh al-Majma': That it is clarified by the conclusion that it was menstruation from the beginning. And more apparent than that is what is in al-Muhit: If he said to his slave 'You are free if you menstruate,' and she said 'I saw blood' and the husband affirmed her, his freedom is not judged until three days continue, then his freedom is judged from the time she saw it, because blood is not menstruation until it continues three days. And the appearance, even if it contains continuation, but the appearance suffices for defense, so the slave uses it to fend off the master's use of him, but it does not suffice for entitlement. So if it continues, it is clarified that it was menstruation, so he is freed from the time she saw the blood, so that if he commits a tort or is injured, his compensation is that of a free person because his freedom is revealed and not retroactive, like the statement 'If so-and-so is in the house, you are free' and it appears at the end of the day, his freedom appears. Unlike his statement 'You are free a month before my death' and he dies after a month and the slave had committed a tort, his ruling is the ruling of slaves according to Abu Hanifa, because there freedom is established retroactively and retroactivity does not apply to what is lost and perished. If the husband says 'The blood stopped within three days' and the woman and slave deny, the word is theirs because the husband has admitted the existence of the condition of freedom outwardly, because the sight of blood in its time is considered menstruation, and therefore she is commanded to leave prayer and fasting. Then he claims an obstacle that makes the blood not menstruation, so he is not believed. If the woman affirms him and the slave denies him within the three days, the word is theirs; if after that, the word is the slave's. And in al-Kafi, there is no alternative to the husband's confirmation in both or evidence in matters that are known. Contemplate. His statement: (And its apparent meaning is that there is no oath upon her) He agreed with it in al-Nahr, and this is apparent in judicial matters. But in matters of conscience, a distinction should be made between menstruation and love, because the conditioning of divorce on her report is only in love. As for menstruation, no, as evidenced by what has passed: that if she is lying in the report, she is divorced in the conditioning on love, but in conditioning on menstruation she is not divorced between her and Allah Almighty, up to the end of what passed. So contemplate. And in Hawashi Miskin, al-Hamawi transmitted from Ramz al-Maqdisi that she must swear an oath by consensus, as this is not among the exceptions from their statement 'Everyone whose statement is accepted must swear an oath.' I say: It is not hidden what is in it, for how, when it has passed that the Law made her trustworthy in what she reports about menstruation and purity, and that what is considered by the Law regarding her is the report of it. Similarly, what follows that if she reports and then retracts, the divorce is not lifted. This is almost explicit in what the author mentioned. Yes, it is restricted in menstruation to judicial matters, not conscience, as you know. Contemplate.

Commentary
This passage explains the timing of a divorce conditioned on menstruation. If the husband says 'You are divorced if you menstruate,' the divorce does not occur upon merely seeing blood because it is not yet confirmed as menstruation (which requires three days continuous bleeding). Only after three days does it become clear that it was menstruation from the start, so the divorce is effective from the first sighting. This is not retroactive but clarificatory. An analogy is given with a slave's freedom conditioned on menstruation: the slave is not freed until three days pass, but then freedom dates back. The passage then discusses evidentiary issues: if the husband claims the blood stopped within three days, the wife and slave are believed because they are the ones who reported the blood initially. Finally, the issue of oath is revisited: in court (qada), the wife is believed without oath, but in conscience (diyanah), a distinction is needed: for love-based reports, she is believed; for menstruation, she is not believed in the sense that if she lies, the divorce does not take effect before Allah. This is a nuanced point emphasizing that the accepted report operates in the worldly court but not necessarily in the divine realm.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Menstrual blood; legally, it requires three consecutive days to be confirmed as hayd.
استمرار (istimrar): Continuity of bleeding for the required period.
التبيين (al-tabyin): Clarification; the nature of the ruling that the divorce is revealed to have been effective from the start, not retroactively created.
القضاء (al-qada): Judicial setting; court rulings based on apparent evidence.
الديانة (al-diyanah): Conscience; what a person knows to be true before Allah, as opposed to court rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately upon her sighting blood.
B) After three days of continuous bleeding, retroactive to the first sighting.
C) Only after the wife confirms it with an oath.
D) At the end of the menstrual period.

Key Takeaway
A divorce conditioned on menstruation is only effective after three continuous days of bleeding, clarifying that it was menstruation from the start.

Conditional Divorce Tied to Menstruation: When It Occurs and Who Is Believed
Expanding rulings on conditional divorce tied to menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that conditional divorce based on menstruation occurs only after three days of continuous bleeding and explain the difference between tabayyun and istinad.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce tied to menstruation only occurs after three days of continuous bleeding, and even then it is retroactive from the first sighting.

Imagine a husband says to his wife, 'You are divorced in your menses.' She sees blood for one day and thinks she is divorced. But according to the Hanafi school, she is not divorced until her blood flows for three continuous days. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the legal reasoning behind this rule, and also who is believed if there is a dispute about the duration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The husband conditions divorce on the wife's menstruation. The issue is when exactly the divorce takes effect: immediately upon seeing blood or after a certain duration.
2
State the Hanafi position
The divorce does not occur upon sighting blood; it only occurs if the blood continues for three full days and nights. When it does, the divorce is retroactive from the first sighting.
This is called tabayyun (clarification), not istinad (retroactive assignment).
3
Understand the proof
Blood is not legally considered menses until it continues for three days (istimrar). The apparent evidence (zahir) suffices for defense (daf) but not for entitlement (istihqaq) until confirmed by continuation.
The proof is based on the Hanafi definition of hayd, which requires a minimum of three days.
4
Distinguish tabayyun from istinad
Tabayyun reveals that the condition was present from the beginning; istinad creates a new legal reality retroactively. In the slave example from al-Muhit, freedom is clarified (tabayyun), whereas in Abu Hanifa's view on 'You are free a month before my death', it is retroactive assignment (istinad) which does not affect lost or perished matters.
This distinction affects rulings on acts that occurred in the interim, such as crimes or marriages.
5
Apply the evidentiary rule
The wife's statement about her menstruation is accepted in qada' (judicial context) without an oath, because the Law made her trustworthy. However, in diyana (before God), she must be truthful. If she later retracts, her retraction is not accepted due to contradiction.
The husband's claim that blood stopped within three days is not believed if the wife and slave deny it, because he affirmed the condition apparently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

حيضك أو إن حضت لأنه لم يتحقق كونه حيضاً حينئذ، فإذا استمر حينئذ ثلاثة أيام بلياليها وقع الطلاق من حين رأت الدم لأنه بالامتداد تبين أنه حيض من الابتداء فيجب على المفتي أن يعينه فيقول طلقت من حين رأت المولى عن نفسه ولا يكفي للاستحقاق فإذا استمر تبين أنه كان حيضاً فيعتق من حين رأت الدم حتى لو جنى أو جنى عليه كان أرشه أرش الأحرار لأنه يظهر عتقه ولا يستند بمنزلة قوله إن كان فلان في الدار فأنت حر فظهر ذلك في آخر النهار يظهر عتقه بخلاف قوله أنت حر قبل موتي بشهر فمات بعده بشهر وقد جنى العبد كان حكمه حكم العبيد عند ا أبي حنيفة لأن ثمة العتق يثبت مستنداً والاستناد لا يظهر في حق الفائت والمتلاشي ، فإن قال الزوج انقطع الدم في الثلاثة وأنكرت المرأة والعبد فالقول لهما لأن الزوج أقر بوجود شرط العتق ظاهراً لأن رؤية الدم في وقته يكون حيضاً ولهذا تؤمر بترك الصلاة والصوم ثم ادعى عارضاً يخرج المرئي من أن يكون حيضاً فلا يصدق فإن صدقته المرأة وكذبه العبد في الأيام الثلاثة فالقول لهما وإن كان بعدها فالقول للعبد اهـ. وفي الكافي بد من تصديق الزوج فيهما أو البينة فيما يثبت بها من الأمر الذي يعلم تأمل. قوله : (وظاهره أنه لا يمين عليها أقره عليه في النهر وهذا في القضاء ظاهر، وأما في الديانة فينبغي التفرقة بين الحيض والمحبة لأن تعلق الطلاق بإخبارها إنما هو في المحبة، أما في الحيض فلا ويدل عليه ما مر من أنها إن كانت كاذبة في الإخبار تطلق في التعليق بالمحبة وفي التعليق بالحيض لا تطلق فيما بينه وبين تعالى إلى آخر ما مر فتدبر وفي حواشي مسكين نقل الحموي عن رمز المقدسي أن عليها اليمين بالإجماع إذ ليس هذا من المواضع المستثناة من قولهم كل من قبل قوله فعليه اليمين اهـ. قلت: ولا يخفى ما فيه كيف وقد مر أن الشرع جعلها أمينة فيما تخبر به عن الحيض والطهر وأن المنظور إليه شرعاً في حقها الإخبار به وكذا ما يأتي من أنها لو اخبرت ثم رجعت لا يرتفع الطلاق فإن هذا كالصريح فيما ذكره المؤلف ذكره المؤلف، نعم يقيد في الحيض بالقضاء لا الديانة لما علمت تأمل . فقالت في مسألة إن حضت فعبدي حر وضرتك طالق إذا رأت الدم فقالت حضت وصدقها أنه قبل الاستمرار يمنع الزوج عن وطء المرأة واستخدام العبد في الثلاثة لاحتمال الاستمرار، فلو صدقها الزوج ثم قالت كان الطهر قبل الدم عشرة أيام لم تصدق لأنه بعد إقرارها بالحيض رجوع بخلافه بعد إقرارها برؤية الدم ولو ادعى الزوج أن الدم كان قبله الطهر عشرة أيام وقالت بل عشرين فالقول لها ولو قال وهي حائض إن طهرت فعبدي حر فقالت طهرت بعد ثلاثة أيام وكذبها الزوج لا يعتق وإن صدقها أو مضت العشرة عتق، وإن قالت بعد العشرة عاودني الدم في العشرة وصدقها الزوج وكذبها العبد عتق، وكذا لو قالت ذلك بعدما أقرت بالانقطاع. وإن كان حيضها خمسة فقال لها إن حضت هذه المرة ستة فعبدي حر رأيت الدم في اليوم السادس إلى آخر اليوم وكذبها الزوج فالقول له لإنكاره شرط العتق بخلاف ما إذا علق عتقه بأصل الحيض فادعى الزوج الانقطاع في الثلاث وادعت الامتداد فالقول لها، وإن صدقها الزوج بالدم في اليوم السادس توقف العتق فإن جاوز العشرة تبين أنه لم يكن حيضاً ولم يعتق وإن لم يجاوز عتق فإن مضت فادعت الانقطاع فيها وادعى المجاوزة فالقول له ولا عتق ولو أخبرت في العشرة بالانقطاع ثم قالت عاودني الدم لا يقبل قولها وإن صدقها الزوج، ولو كانت عادتها خمسة فطلقها في مرض موته فحاضت حيضتين الثالثة ثم مات الزوج في بعد خمسة فقالت الورثة طهرت على رأس الخمسة ولا ميراث لك وقالت لم ينقطع وأرى الدم في الحال فالقول لها لأن الأصل في كل ثابت دوامه فهي تتمسك بهذا الظاهر لدفع الحرمان وهو حجة للدفع وتمامه في الكافي. ومن أحكام الوقوع من الابتداء أنها لو كانت غير مدخولة وتزوجت حين رأت الدم فإن النكاح صحيح. ومن أحكامه أنها لا تحسب هذه الحيضة من العدة لأنها بعض حيضة لأنه حين كان الشرط رؤية الدم لزم أن يقع الطلاق بعد حيضها. وفي الخانية : رجل قال لامرأته قبل الدخول إذا حضت فأنت طالق فقالت حضت وتزوجت من ساعتها ثم مات قال محمد ميراثها للزوج الأول دون الثاني. وقال : لا يدري أكان ذلك حيضاً أو لا ا هـ. ومن أحكامه أيضاً أن الطلاق بدعي. ومنها أنه لو خالعها في الثلاث بطل الخلع لكونها مطلقة ذكرهما في الجوهرة. وفي الثاني نظر لأن الخلع يلحق قوله : (ثم قالت كان الطهر قبل الدم عشرة أيام أي فلا يكون هذا الدم حيضاً لأن أقل الطهر الفاصل بين الحيضتين خمسة عشر يوماً وقوله بخلافه بعد إقرارها برؤية الدم أي إذا قالت رأيت الدم ولم تقل حضت ثم قالت كان الطهر عشرة أيام فإنها تصدق لأن قولها رأيت الدم ليس إقراراً بالحيض فلم يكن ذلك رجوعاً عن إقرارها قوله: (وفي الثاني نظر الخ) قال في النهر : الظاهر أنه محمول على ما إذا لم تكن مدخولاً بها وعليه فلا إشكال.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Conditional Divorce. It occurs; if it continues for three [days] it occurs from the time she saw [blood]; and if [he says] 'If you menstruate one menses' it occurs when she becomes pure; and if [he says] 'If by her statement' and the oath is to hope for refusal. And if she then says 'I was lying' the divorce is not removed due to her contradiction, as will be cited from al-Kafi shortly, God willing. His statement: 'And by seeing blood it does not occur; if it continues for three [days] it occurs from the time she saw' means it does not occur by seeing it in the case where he conditioned divorce on her menses, whether with 'if' or 'in' or 'with', such as 'You are divorced in your menses' or 'with your menses' or 'if you menstruate', because it is not verified that it is menses at that time. So if it continues for three days and their nights, the divorce occurs from the time she saw blood, because by extension it becomes clear that it was menses from the beginning. Therefore, the mufti must specify and say: 'She is divorced from the time she saw blood.' This is not from the chapter of istinad but from the chapter of tabayyun, hence he said 'from the time she saw'. The author said in Sharh al-Majma': It becomes clear by the end that it was menses from the beginning. More explicit than that is what is in al-Muhit: If he said to her 'Your slave is free if you menstruate' and she said 'I saw blood' and the husband believed her, it is not judged that he is free until it continues for three days, then it is judged that he is free from the time she saw, because blood is not menses until it continues for three days. The apparent [evidence] even if it involves continuation, but the apparent suffices for defense; so the slave uses it to repel the master's using him for service, but it does not suffice for entitlement. So if it continues, it becomes clear that it was menses, so he is free from the time she saw blood, such that if he commits an offense or an offense is committed against him, his indemnity is the indemnity of free men, because his freedom becomes apparent and is not based on istinad. Like his saying 'If so-and-so is in the house then you are free', and it appears at the end of the day, his freedom appears. Contrary to his saying 'You are free a month before my death' and he dies after a month and the slave has committed an offense, his ruling is the ruling of slaves according to Abu Hanifa, because there the freedom is established by istinad and istinad does not appear in respect of what is lost and perished. If the husband said 'The blood stopped within the three [days]' and the woman and the slave denied, then the statement is for them, because the husband affirmed the existence of the condition of freedom apparently, because seeing blood at its time is menses, and for this she is commanded to leave prayer and fasting; then he claims an impediment that removes the seen from being menses, so he is not believed. If the woman believed him and the slave contradicted him within the three days, the statement is for them; if after them, the statement is for the slave. End. In al-Kafi: It is necessary to believe the husband in both or [provide] evidence regarding what is established by it from a matter that is known. Reflect. His statement: 'And apparently there is no oath upon her' he affirmed it in al-Nahr, and this in qada' is apparent; but in diyana, it is appropriate to differentiate between menses and love, because the attachment of divorce to her statement is only in love; as for menses, it is not. This is indicated by what has passed: if she is lying in the statement, she is divorced in the conditioning on love, but in the conditioning on menses, she is not divorced between him and God Almighty, etc. So reflect. In the marginal notes of Miskin, al-Hamawi transmitted from Ramz al-Maqdisi that upon her is the oath by consensus, since this is not from the exceptions of the statement 'Everyone whose statement is accepted must take an oath.' End. I say: It is not hidden what is in it; how? And it has passed that the Law made her trustworthy in what she reports about menses and purity, and that what is considered in shari'a regarding her is the statement, and likewise what comes later: if she then retracts, the divorce is not removed. This is as clear as possible regarding what the author mentioned. Indeed, it is restricted regarding menses to qada', not diyana, for what you know. Reflect. She said in the issue: If I menstruate, my slave is free and your co-wife is divorced, when she saw blood and said 'I menstruated' and he believed her that before continuation he prevents the husband from intercourse with the woman and using the slave in the three [days] due to the possibility of continuation. So if the husband believed her, then she said 'the purity before the blood was ten days', she is not believed because after her affirmation of menses it is a retraction, contrary to after her affirmation of seeing blood. If the husband claimed that the blood was preceded by purity of ten days and she said 'twenty', the statement is for her. If he said while she was menstruating 'If you become pure, my slave is free' and she said 'I became pure after three days' and the husband contradicted her, he is not free; if he believed her or ten days passed, he is free. If she said after ten days 'The blood returned during the ten' and the husband believed her and the slave contradicted her, he is free; likewise if she said that after she had affirmed the cessation. If her menses was five [days] and he said to her 'If you menstruate this time six [days], my slave is free' and he saw blood on the sixth day to the end of the day and the husband contradicted her, the statement is for him because he denies the condition of freedom; contrary to if he conditioned his freedom on the original menses, then the husband claimed cessation within the three and she claimed extension, the statement is for her. If the husband believed her regarding the blood on the sixth day, freedom is suspended; if it exceeds ten, it becomes clear that it was not menses and he is not free; if it does not exceed, he is free. If she then claimed cessation within it and he claimed extension, the statement is for him and no freedom. If she reported within the ten [days] the cessation, then said 'the blood returned', her statement is not accepted even if the husband believed her. If her custom was five, and he divorced her in his death illness, she menstruated two menses, then the third, then the husband died after five, and the heirs said 'You became pure at the end of five, no inheritance for you', and she said 'It did not stop and I see blood now', the statement is for her because the principle is that everything established remains, so she clings to this apparent to repel deprivation, and it is an argument for defense. Its completion is in al-Kafi. From the rulings of occurrence from the beginning: If she was not consummated and married when she saw the blood, the marriage is valid. And from its rulings: This menses is not counted from the 'idda because it is part of a menses, since when the condition was seeing the blood, it necessarily means that the divorce occurs after her menses. In al-Khaniyya: A man said to his wife before consummation 'If you menstruate, you are divorced.' She said 'I menstruated' and married immediately, then he died. Muhammad said: Her inheritance is for the first husband, not the second. He said: It is not known whether that was menses or not. End. And from its rulings also: The divorce is bid'i. And from them: If he made khula' with her during the three, the khula' is invalid because she is divorced. Both mentioned in al-Jawhara. And the second has consideration because khula' participates. His statement: 'Then she said the purity before the blood was ten days' means this blood is not menses because the minimum purity between two menses is fifteen days. And his statement: 'Contrary to after her affirmation of seeing blood' means if she said 'I saw blood' and did not say 'I menstruated', then said 'the purity was ten days', she is believed because her statement 'I saw blood' is not an affirmation of menses, so it is not a retraction of her affirmation. His statement: 'And the second has consideration' al-Nahr said: Apparently it is carried on when she is not consummated, and accordingly there is no issue.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion (sharh) on the ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) when tied to menstruation. The author begins by establishing a key principle: if a husband says to his wife, 'You are divorced in your menses' or similar, the divorce does not occur merely by seeing blood. It only occurs if the blood continues for three full days (including nights), and when it does, the divorce is retroactively effective from the moment the blood was first seen. This is called tabayyun (clarification) rather than istinad (retroactive assignment). The mufti must specify this retroactive effect.

The text then presents a case from al-Muhit involving a slave's freedom conditioned on the wife's menstruation. If the wife says she saw blood and the husband believes her, the slave is not freed immediately; only after three days of continuous blood does freedom become effective retroactively. This demonstrates that the apparent evidence (al-zahir) suffices for defense (daf' al-darar) but not for entitlement (istihqaq) until confirmed.

The discussion then turns to conflicts of testimony. If the husband claims the blood stopped within three days but the wife and slave deny, the word of the wife and slave is accepted because the husband had already affirmed the condition apparently. If the wife later claims the purity period was ten days (which would make the blood non-menstrual), she is not believed after affirming menses, but if she had only said 'I saw blood' without affirming menses, her subsequent claim about purity is accepted as it is not a retraction.

There is a notable intra-Hanafi point: the author quotes that according to Abu Hanifa, in cases of retroactive freedom (istinad), such as saying 'You are free a month before my death', if the slave commits an offense before the death, he is still treated as a slave for that offense because retroactivity does not apply to what is lost. This contrasts with the case of conditional freedom upon menstruation where freedom is merely clarified (tabayyun), not retroactively assigned.

The text also addresses the issue of oaths: apparently no oath is required from the wife about her statement, but this is limited to qada' (judicial context); in diyana (before God), there is a nuance. Some scholars hold that an oath is required by consensus, but the author favors the view that she is considered trustworthy regarding menses due to the shari'a making her an aminah.

Later, various scenarios from al-Kafi are presented, including cases where the husband conditions freeing a slave on the wife's menstruation of six days while her habit is five, etc. In each, the principle of apparent evidence and the burden of proof for defense vs. entitlement is applied.

Finally, the text lists rulings that result from the divorce occurring from the beginning: a non-consummated wife who marries immediately after seeing blood has a valid marriage; this menses is not counted in the 'idda because it is only part of a menses; the divorce is classified as bid'i (innovative) because it occurs during menses; and if khula' was made during the three days, it is invalid because she was already divorced. There is a consideration regarding khula' that it participates, but al-Nahr resolves it by assuming the case is of a non-consummated marriage.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Conditional attachment of a divorce to a future event.
حيض (hayd): Menstrual blood that flows continuously for at least three days and nights.
تبيين (tabayyun): Clarification that a condition was actually present from the beginning, as opposed to retroactive assignment (istinad).
استمرار (istimrar): Continuity of blood flow for three days.
استناد (istinad): Retroactive assignment of a legal effect to a past time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced in your menses' and she sees blood that lasts only two days, what is the ruling?
A) She is divorced immediately upon seeing blood.
B) She is not divorced because the blood did not last three days.
C) She is divorced after two days.
D) The divorce occurs only if she confirms it with an oath.

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce tied to menstruation, the divorce only takes effect after three days of continuous bleeding, retroactively from the first sighting; the wife's statement about menses is accepted in qada' without oath.

Testimony of Menstruation: Credibility and Retraction in Conditional Divorce
Expansion of conditional divorce rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for when a wife's claim of menstruation is accepted in conditional divorce and when retraction is not allowed.

Core Idea
A wife's claim of hayd is provisional until confirmed by three days; retraction after acknowledging hayd is rejected, but not after merely seeing blood.

In conditional divorce linked to menstruation, the wife's testimony about her state is crucial but subject to rules of credibility and continuity. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rulings on when her word is accepted, when retraction is allowed, and how the husband's confirmation or denial affects the outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and parties' claims
The husband says 'If you menstruate, my slave is free.' The wife claims she saw blood and menstruated. Understand that the condition triggers emancipation only if true menstruation (minimum 3 days) occurs. The wife's initial claim does not automatically fulfill the condition.
The condition is suspended on the occurrence of hayd, not merely seeing blood.
2
Apply the continuity provisional rule
Before the blood continues for three days, the husband is prevented from intercourse and using the slave due to the possibility it may not be hayd. The law suspends effects until continuity is established.
This is based on the principle that hayd must last at least three days (the minimum).
3
Analyze the retraction rules
If the wife acknowledges menstruation (says 'I menstruated') then later claims purity was shorter than required, she is not believed because she made a legal admission (iqrar). But if she only said 'I saw blood,' her later claim of short purity is accepted because seeing blood is not an acknowledgment of hayd.
Hanafi law treats iqrar as binding; retraction is only possible if the admission is not conclusive.
4
Compare scenarios of disagreement on days
When husband and wife disagree on the number of days of purity before blood or the length of bleeding, the say (qawl) is given to the wife in some cases and the husband in others, based on who denies the condition for emancipation.
Example: If husband denies the condition (e.g., claims blood didn't last six days), his say prevails regarding the condition's fulfillment.
5
Apply istishab (continuity) to inheritance case
In the death-illness divorce scenario, the wife's claim that she is still menstruating is accepted because the original state (being in hayd) continues until proven otherwise. Istishab preserves the existing state to ward off deprivation of inheritance.
This is a proof (hujja) for her claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب التعليق
فقالت
في مسألة إن حضت فعبدي حر وضرتك طالق إذا رأت الدم فقالت حضت وصدقها أنه قبل الاستمرار يمنع الزوج عن وطء المرأة واستخدام العبد في الثلاثة لاحتمال الاستمرار، فلو صدقها الزوج ثم قالت كان الطهر قبل الدم عشرة أيام لم تصدق لأنه بعد إقرارها بالحيض رجوع بخلافه بعد إقرارها برؤية الدم ولو ادعى الزوج أن الدم كان قبله الطهر عشرة أيام وقالت بل عشرين فالقول لها ولو قال وهي حائض إن طهرت فعبدي حر فقالت طهرت بعد ثلاثة أيام وكذبها الزوج لا يعتق وإن صدقها أو مضت العشرة عتق، وإن قالت بعد العشرة عاودني الدم في العشرة وصدقها الزوج وكذبها العبد عتق، وكذا لو قالت ذلك بعدما أقرت بالانقطاع. وإن كان حيضها خمسة فقال لها إن حضت هذه المرة ستة فعبدي حر رأيت الدم في اليوم السادس إلى آخر اليوم وكذبها الزوج فالقول له لإنكاره شرط العتق بخلاف ما إذا علق عتقه بأصل الحيض فادعى الزوج الانقطاع في الثلاث وادعت الامتداد فالقول لها، وإن صدقها الزوج بالدم في اليوم السادس توقف العتق فإن جاوز العشرة تبين أنه لم يكن حيضاً ولم يعتق وإن لم يجاوز عتق فإن مضت فادعت الانقطاع فيها وادعى المجاوزة فالقول له ولا عتق ولو أخبرت في العشرة بالانقطاع ثم قالت عاودني الدم لا يقبل قولها وإن صدقها الزوج، ولو كانت عادتها خمسة فطلقها في مرض موته فحاضت حيضتين الثالثة ثم مات الزوج في بعد خمسة فقالت الورثة طهرت على رأس الخمسة ولا ميراث لك وقالت لم ينقطع وأرى الدم في الحال فالقول لها لأن الأصل في كل ثابت دوامه فهي تتمسك بهذا الظاهر لدفع الحرمان وهو حجة للدفع وتمامه في الكافي. ومن أحكام الوقوع من الابتداء أنها لو كانت غير مدخولة وتزوجت حين رأت الدم فإن النكاح صحيح. ومن أحكامه أنها لا تحسب هذه الحيضة من العدة لأنها بعض حيضة لأنه حين كان الشرط رؤية الدم لزم أن يقع الطلاق بعد حيضها.
وفي الخانية : رجل قال لامرأته قبل الدخول إذا حضت فأنت طالق فقالت حضت وتزوجت من ساعتها ثم مات قال محمد ميراثها للزوج الأول دون الثاني. وقال : لا يدري أكان ذلك حيضاً أو لا ا هـ. ومن أحكامه أيضاً أن الطلاق بدعي. ومنها أنه لو خالعها في الثلاث بطل الخلع لكونها مطلقة ذكرهما في الجوهرة. وفي الثاني نظر لأن الخلع يلحق
قوله : (ثم قالت كان الطهر قبل الدم عشرة أيام أي فلا يكون هذا الدم حيضاً لأن أقل الطهر الفاصل بين الحيضتين خمسة عشر يوماً وقوله بخلافه بعد إقرارها برؤية الدم أي إذا قالت رأيت الدم ولم تقل حضت ثم قالت كان الطهر عشرة أيام فإنها تصدق لأن قولها رأيت الدم ليس إقراراً بالحيض فلم يكن ذلك رجوعاً عن إقرارها قوله: (وفي الثاني نظر الخ) قال في النهر : الظاهر أنه محمول على ما إذا لم تكن مدخولاً بها وعليه فلا إشكال.
[Book of Divorce, Chapter of Conditional Divorce] She said: In the issue: If you menstruate, my slave is free and your co-wife is divorced, when she sees blood and says 'I menstruated' and he confirms her, before continuity it prevents the husband from intercourse with the woman and using the slave in the three [days] due to possibility of continuity. If the husband confirms her, then she says 'the purity before the blood was ten days,' she is not believed because after her acknowledgment of menstruation it is a retraction, contrary to after her acknowledgment of seeing blood. If the husband claims the blood had before it ten days of purity and she says rather twenty, the say is hers. If he says while she is menstruating 'if you become pure, my slave is free,' and she says 'I became pure after three days' and the husband denies her, he is not emancipated; if he confirms her or ten days pass, he is emancipated. If she says after ten days 'the blood returned to me within the ten' and the husband confirms her and the slave denies her, he is emancipated. Likewise if she says that after she acknowledged cessation. If her menstruation is five, and he said to her 'If you menstruate this time six, my slave is free,' she saw blood on the sixth day to the end of the day, and the husband denies her, the say is his because he denies the condition of emancipation, contrary to if he attached emancipation to the origin of menstruation, so the husband claims cessation in the three and she claims extension, the say is hers. If the husband confirms her blood on the sixth day, emancipation is suspended; if it exceeds ten, it becomes clear it was not menstruation and he is not emancipated; if it does not exceed, he is emancipated. If it passes and she claims cessation within it and he claims excess, the say is his and no emancipation. If she reports cessation within the ten then says 'the blood returned to me,' her saying is not accepted even if the husband confirms her. If her custom is five, and he divorces her in his death illness, then she menstruates two menstruations, a third, then the husband dies after five, and the heirs say 'She became pure at the end of five and no inheritance for you,' and she says 'It did not cease and I see blood now,' the say is hers because the principle for every established thing is its continuity; she adheres to this apparent to ward off deprivation, and it is a proof for warding off. Its completion is in al-Kafi. Among the rulings of occurrence from the beginning: if she was not entered upon and she married when she saw the blood, the marriage is valid. Among its rulings: this menstrual period is not counted from the 'iddah because it is part of a menstrual period, because when the condition was seeing blood, it necessitated that divorce falls after her menstruation. In al-Khaniyya: A man said to his wife before consummation 'If you menstruate, you are divorced,' and she said 'I menstruated' and married immediately, then he died. Muhammad said: Her inheritance is for the first husband, not the second. And he said: It is not known whether that was menstruation or not. End. Among its rulings also: the divorce is bid'i. Among them: if he made khul' with her in the three, the khul' is invalid because she is divorced, mentioned in al-Jawhara. In the second there is consideration because khul' attaches to... His saying: (Then she said the purity before the blood was ten days) meaning this blood is not menstruation because the minimum purity separating two menstruations is fifteen days. And his saying: contrary to after her acknowledgment of seeing blood, meaning if she said 'I saw blood' and did not say 'I menstruated,' then said 'the purity was ten days,' she is believed because her saying 'I saw blood' is not an acknowledgment of menstruation, so that was not a retraction of her acknowledgment. His saying: (And in the second there is consideration etc.) He said in al-Nahr: The apparent is that it is carried on if she was not entered upon, and thus there is no problem.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion of rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq on conditional divorce linked to menstruation. It opens by presenting a case where a husband says 'If you menstruate, my slave is free and your co-wife is divorced.' The wife claims she saw blood and menstruated. If the husband confirms her, before the blood is confirmed as hayd by continuity (lasting three days), he is prevented from intercourse and using the slave due to possibility that the blood may not be true menstruation. The author then discusses retraction: if after confirming she later says her purity before the blood was ten days (which would negate hayd because the minimum purity is fifteen days), she is not believed because she already acknowledged hayd. But if she only said she saw blood, not that she menstruated, her subsequent claim of ten-day purity is accepted. In cases of disagreement about the length of purity before blood, or the number of days of bleeding, the say is given to the woman in some scenarios and to the husband in others, based on who is denying the condition for emancipation. The text also covers the scenario where a woman's habit is five days but condition is set for six; if she sees blood on the sixth day and husband denies, the husband's say prevails because he denies the condition. If he confirms, emancipation is suspended until it is known whether the bleeding exceeds ten days. The passage also addresses inheritance in a death-illness divorce, where the principle of continuity (istishab) is applied to accept the woman's claim that she is still menstruating, inheriting from her husband. Later, rulings about the validity of marriage if she is non-madkhula and marries immediately, and that this type of divorce is bid'i (innovative). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute from al-Khaniyya where Muhammad holds inheritance goes to first husband, and a comment that khul' during the three days is invalid because she is already divorced. The commentary then explains the matn: the wife's retraction is not accepted after she says 'I menstruated' but is accepted if she only said 'I saw blood'. The passage ends by noting a consideration on the second point about khul'.

Key Terms
حيض (ḥayḍ): Menstrual blood that triggers legal rulings; minimum three days, maximum ten; used as condition for divorce.
تعليق (taʿlīq): Conditional attachment of a legal effect (like divorce or emancipation) to a future event.
إقرار (iqrār): Acknowledgment; in this context, the wife's acknowledgment of having menstruated or seen blood.
استصحاب (istiṣḥāb): Presumption of continuity of an existing state until proven otherwise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the conditional divorce scenario, if the wife says 'I saw blood' but does not say 'I menstruated,' and later says 'the purity before the blood was ten days,' is she believed?
A) No, because seeing blood implies menstruation.
B) Yes, because she did not acknowledge menstruation, so her retraction is accepted.
C) Only if the husband confirms her.
D) Only if the ten days pass without further bleeding.

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce, the wife's testimony about hayd is provisional; retraction is only possible if she did not legally acknowledge hayd.
