Book of Divorce, Chapter of Conditional Divorce: He stipulated for occurrence the existence of ownership at the end of them both. Likewise, if it is an act undertaken by two persons in terms of being undertaken by them, such as 'if Zaid and Amr come,' then likewise the condition is their coming. If this is known, then the commentator limited the author's speech to the second section in a way that is not appropriate. And the objection of al-Kamal to the commentator for making the issue of speech from multiplicity of condition is an oversight, because he only made it from the type of condition comprising two attributes, and upon it he interpreted the author's expression, not from the type of multiplicity of condition. The summary is that if he repeats the conditional particle without conjunction, then occurrence depends on both (conditions) existing, whether he puts the consequence before them or after them or in between. However, if he puts it before or after, ownership is required at the time of the last of them, which is the first uttered in terms of precedence or delay; if he puts it in between, then ownership is required at both. If the condition is with conjunction, then it is suspended on one of them if he puts the consequence before or in between; if he puts it after, it is suspended on both. If he does not repeat the conditional particle, then the existence of both things is necessary, whether he puts the consequence before or after them. This is what appears to me from their statements. And in al-Walwalijiyya: If he says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced if you speak to so-and-so,' then the first and second divorce are attached to the first condition, and the third to the second condition, so that if she enters, she is divorced by two divorces; if she speaks to him, she is divorced by one. Not that the first condition becomes a condition of validity for all and the second a condition of dissolution for all, because if we attached the second consequence to entering, the consequence would be delayed after the condition; and if we attached it to speaking, the consequence would be preceded over the condition. The principle for the condition is precedence, so whenever possible to preserve it upon the principle, it is not changed. And if he says to his wife 'You are divorced if you enter the house, and my slave is free, and upon me is walking to the House of Allah if you speak to so-and-so,' then the divorce is on entering, the manumission and walking are on speaking. The intermediate consequence is attached to the last condition here, unlike what preceded, because there the speech is consistent because he joined noun to noun, so connection became the principle; it is only broken for necessity, and there is no necessity regarding the intervening one. Here, the speech is disconnected because he joined noun to verb, so it is not attached to the first except by necessity, because it is possible to attach it to the second. End. The complete branches of divorce suspended on marriage and on speech are mentioned in Tatimmat al-Fatawa, chapter of suspension of divorce on ownership. And in al-Bazzaziyya from al-Ayman: Divorce added to two times falls at the first of them; the one suspended on two actions: the author's speech means if the condition has two attributes etc., and it is apparent that this is from multiple conditions, and the excuse for the commentator is that it is not valid to intend every two conditions because of the objection that if he places the consequence in the middle, then ownership is required for the first of them, unlike every condition having two attributes, for which ownership at the last is valid. So ponder. And it indicates that the author first stated that what is meant by two conditions are two things upon which divorce depends and does not occur by one of them, whether they are two conditions in reality or not. By this generalization he included the speech issue in the author's statement, so what is in the commentary is built upon it. So the author's statement 'not from the type of multiplicity of condition' has scrutiny due to disagreement with what he himself established. And the objection of al-Kamal regarding the three suspensions: and if he suspends the three or manumission on intercourse, the dower is not required by staying, and he does not become... [the rest of the translation omitted for brevity]
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, covering the chapter of conditional divorce (ta'liq). The author, Ibn Nujaym, explains the rulings regarding multiple conditions and the effect of an immediate triple divorce on a suspended triple divorce. The passage begins by discussing a case where the condition is an act involving two persons: possession (kingship) must exist at the end of both conditions. It then clarifies the confusion between a condition comprising two attributes (shart dhu wasfayn) and two separate conditions (ta'addud al-shart). The summary (hasil) explains the rules when the conditional particle is repeated with or without conjunction, specifying when the consequence (jaza') is positioned before, after, or between the conditions. The al-Walwalijiyya example illustrates a scenario where multiple divorce pronouncements are attached to different conditions. The author then discusses the effect of an immediate triple divorce on a suspended divorce: it invalidates the suspension because it destroys the legal capacity (mahall) for the suspended divorce to occur. A distinction is made between divorce, manumission, and zihar; manumission is not invalidated by a later sale because the slave remains in the state of potential emancipation, while zihar is not invalidated because it is a prohibition of an act rather than of the original marital bond. The passage includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad holds that a second husband only removes the effect of the earlier divorces partially, whereas Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (together, 'indahuma) hold that the second husband completely nullifies the earlier divorces. The relied-upon position for fatwa is that an immediate triple divorce voids a suspended triple divorce because it renders the wife absolutely prohibited (hareem ghalizah) and thus removes the possibility of the condition being fulfilled while the wife is in his nikah.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of divorce upon a future condition
الشرط (al-shart): The condition upon which the divorce is suspended
الجزاء (al-jaza'): The consequence or result of the condition, i.e., the divorce pronouncement
تعدد الشرط (ta'addud al-shart): Multiple conditions, where each condition is separately stated

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband repeats the conditional particle without conjunction and places the consequence (jaza') after both conditions, when must ownership (king) exist for the divorce to occur?
A) At the time of the first condition.
B) At the time of the last condition.
C) At the time of both conditions.
D) Ownership is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce, the structure of the condition determines whether both or only one element must occur; the Hanafi rules are precise and depend on repetition, conjunction, and position of the consequence.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
10 min
Effect of Immediate Triple Divorce on Suspended Triple Divorce
Sharh on Kanz al-Daqa'iq: effect of immediate triple divorce on suspended triple divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an immediate triple divorce invalidates a suspended triple divorce, distinguish the rulings for divorce, manumission, and zihar, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the second husband's effect.

Core Idea
An immediate triple divorce destroys the legal capacity for a suspended divorce; a single or double does not.

When a husband suspends triple divorce on a condition and then immediately pronounces triple divorce, the suspension becomes void because the wife becomes absolutely prohibited. This lesson explores the ruling, its reasoning, and how it differs from manumission and zihar. We also examine the disagreement among the Hanafi founders regarding the role of a second husband.

Guided Path
1
State the rule and its scope
The rule: immediate triple divorce (tunjiz al-thalath) invalidates a suspended triple divorce (ta'liq al-thalath). This applies only to triple divorce, not single or double. It also applies to suspended triple divorce specifically, not to other suspended acts.
If the suspended divorce is less than three, the immediate divorce does not void it because the wife remains lawful after 'iddah or after a second husband.
2
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
Divorce is a dissolution of the original marital bond (tahrim al-hal al-asli). Immediate triple divorce renders the wife permanently prohibited (hareem ghalizah). The suspended divorce can only occur while the marriage is valid; once the wife is haram, the condition cannot be fulfilled in a valid marriage. Thus, the suspension becomes impossible and void.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the nature of divorce.
3
Compare with manumission and zihar
Manumission: if a master suspends a slave's freedom on a condition and then sells the slave, the suspension remains valid because the slave's status as a potential free person (mahall for 'itq) is not destroyed by sale. Similarly, zihar (likening wife to mother) is not invalidated by immediate triple divorce because zihar prohibits an act (sexual intercourse), not the original marital bond. The marriage is merely a condition for zihar, not its object.
The distinction: divorce directly attacks the marriage bond; manumission and zihar affect attributes of the subject matter.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
The three founders (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree on the basic rule but differ on the effect of a second husband. Imam Muhammad holds that if the husband immediately divorces two times (not three), and later the wife marries another, divorces, and returns, then the suspended triple divorce results in one additional divorce (total three). The two Sahibs hold that the second husband completely nullifies the earlier divorces, so the suspended triple divorce falls entirely.
The passage mentions that if the suspended divorce is triple and the immediate is less than three, the suspension remains valid and can be fulfilled after the second husband.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that immediate triple divorce voids a suspended triple divorce. The dispute among the founders is noted but not decisive for the core rule. For practical purposes, if a man immediately divorces three times, any suspended divorce is null.
The author, Ibn Nujaym, seems to lean towards the view that immediate triple voids the suspension, as it is the majority view.

Book of Divorce, Chapter of Conditional Divorce: He stipulated for occurrence the existence of ownership at the end of them both. Likewise, if it is an act undertaken by two persons in terms of being undertaken by them, such as 'if Zaid and Amr come,' then likewise the condition is their coming. If this is known, then the commentator limited the author's speech to the second section in a way that is not appropriate. And the objection of al-Kamal to the commentator for making the issue of speech from multiplicity of condition is an oversight, because he only made it from the type of condition comprising two attributes, and upon it he interpreted the author's expression, not from the type of multiplicity of condition. The summary is that if he repeats the conditional particle without conjunction, then occurrence depends on both (conditions) existing, whether he puts the consequence before them or after them or in between. However, if he puts it before or after, ownership is required at the time of the last of them, which is the first uttered in terms of precedence or delay; if he puts it in between, then ownership is required at both. If the condition is with conjunction, then it is suspended on one of them if he puts the consequence before or in between; if he puts it after, it is suspended on both. If he does not repeat the conditional particle, then the existence of both things is necessary, whether he puts the consequence before or after them. This is what appears to me from their statements. And in al-Walwalijiyya: If he says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and divorced, and divorced if you speak to so-and-so,' then the first and second divorce are attached to the first condition, and the third to the second condition, so that if she enters, she is divorced by two divorces; if she speaks to him, she is divorced by one. Not that the first condition becomes a condition of validity for all and the second a condition of dissolution for all, because if we attached the second consequence to entering, the consequence would be delayed after the condition; and if we attached it to speaking, the consequence would be preceded over the condition. The principle for the condition is precedence, so whenever possible to preserve it upon the principle, it is not changed. And if he says to his wife 'You are divorced if you enter the house, and my slave is free, and upon me is walking to the House of Allah if you speak to so-and-so,' then the divorce is on entering, the manumission and walking are on speaking. The intermediate consequence is attached to the last condition here, unlike what preceded, because there the speech is consistent because he joined noun to noun, so connection became the principle; it is only broken for necessity, and there is no necessity regarding the intervening one. Here, the speech is disconnected because he joined noun to verb, so it is not attached to the first except by necessity, because it is possible to attach it to the second. End. The complete branches of divorce suspended on marriage and on speech are mentioned in Tatimmat al-Fatawa, chapter of suspension of divorce on ownership. And in al-Bazzaziyya from al-Ayman: Divorce added to two times falls at the first of them; the one suspended on two actions: the author's speech means if the condition has two attributes etc., and it is apparent that this is from multiple conditions, and the excuse for the commentator is that it is not valid to intend every two conditions because of the objection that if he places the consequence in the middle, then ownership is required for the first of them, unlike every condition having two attributes, for which ownership at the last is valid. So ponder. And it indicates that the author first stated that what is meant by two conditions are two things upon which divorce depends and does not occur by one of them, whether they are two conditions in reality or not. By this generalization he included the speech issue in the author's statement, so what is in the commentary is built upon it. So the author's statement 'not from the type of multiplicity of condition' has scrutiny due to disagreement with what he himself established. And the objection of al-Kamal regarding the three suspensions: and if he suspends the three or manumission on intercourse, the dower is not required by staying, and he does not become... Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, covering the chapter of conditional divorce (ta'liq). The author, Ibn Nujaym, explains the rulings regarding multiple conditions and the effect of an immediate triple divorce on a suspended triple divorce. The passage begins by discussing a case where the condition is an act involving two persons: possession (kingship) must exist at the end of both conditions. It then clarifies the confusion between a condition comprising two attributes (shart dhu wasfayn) and two separate conditions (ta'addud al-shart). The summary (hasil) explains the rules when the conditional particle is repeated with or without conjunction, specifying when the consequence (jaza') is positioned before, after, or between the conditions. The al-Walwalijiyya example illustrates a scenario where multiple divorce pronouncements are attached to different conditions. The author then discusses the effect of an immediate triple divorce on a suspended divorce: it invalidates the suspension because it destroys the legal capacity (mahall) for the suspended divorce to occur. A distinction is made between divorce, manumission, and zihar; manumission is not invalidated by a later sale because the slave remains in the state of potential emancipation, while zihar is not invalidated because it is a prohibition of an act rather than of the original marital bond. The passage includes an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad holds that a second husband only removes the effect of the earlier divorces partially, whereas Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (together, 'indahuma) hold that the second husband completely nullifies the earlier divorces. The relied-upon position for fatwa is that an immediate triple divorce voids a suspended triple divorce because it renders the wife absolutely prohibited (hareem ghalizah) and thus removes the possibility of the condition being fulfilled while the wife is in his nikah.

Key Terms
تنجيز الثلاث (tunjiz al-thalath): Immediate pronouncement of triple divorce without condition
المحلية (al-mahalliyya): The legal capacity or subject matter that can receive the effect of the suspended divorce
الهدم (al-hadm): The dismantling effect of a second marriage on previous divorces
الظهار (al-zihar): A declaration likening one's wife to a prohibited female relative, making sexual intercourse prohibited until expiation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is correct regarding the effect of an immediate triple divorce on a suspended triple divorce?
A) The suspension is voided because the immediate triple destroys the legal capacity.
B) The suspension remains valid because a second husband can revive it.
C) The suspension is voided only if the wife remarries and returns.
D) The suspension is transformed into a single divorce.

Key Takeaway
An immediate triple divorce voids a suspended triple divorce because it permanently destroys the marriage bond, making the condition impossible to fulfill; this distinguishes divorce from manumission and zihar.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
8 min
Immediate Pronouncement of Three Invalidates Conditional Divorce
Ruling on invalidation of ta'liq by tanziz of three divorces with conditions and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that immediate pronouncement of three divorces invalidates a suspended conditional divorce, distinguish between tanziz of three and less, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a second marriage.

Core Idea
Immediate pronouncement of three divorces voids a conditional divorce; less than three does not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the important Hanafi principle that the immediate pronouncement (tanziz) of three divorces invalidates a suspended conditional divorce (ta'liq). The ruling is nuanced: only a triple immediate divorce has this effect; one or two immediate divorces do not nullify the condition. The text also delves into intra-Hanafi disagreements about scenarios involving remarriage after separation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
Determine what action invalidates the suspended conditional divorce. The text states: immediate pronouncement of three divorces (tanziz al-thalath) invalidates the ta'liq.
The word 'yubtil' means 'invalidates', indicating that the suspension becomes void.
2
Understand the Limitation to Three
Only tanziz of three divorces has this effect. If the husband pronounces one or two divorces immediately, the suspension remains valid because the capacity (mahall) for divorce partially remains.
The restriction to three is explicitly qayyada bil-thalath.
3
Apply to Scenarios with Second Marriage
Consider the case where the husband had suspended three, pronounced two immediately, then after a second marriage the condition occurs. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), all three suspended divorces occur. According to Muhammad alone, only one occurs.
The difference hinges on whether the second husband's consummation demolishes the previous divorces.
4
Distinguish from Emancipation and Zihar
The rule is specific to divorce. Immediate pronouncement of three does not invalidate a suspended emancipation or zihar because those do not affect the original permissibility (hall asli) of the marriage.
This is highlighted by the text's taqyid bil-talaq.

الملك إذا زال بعد تعليق العتق لا يبطل التعليق كما إذا قال لعبده إن دخلت الدار فأنت حر ثم باعه ثم اشتراه ثم دخل عتق لأن العبد بصفة الرق محل للعتق وبالبيع لم تفت تلك الصفة حتى لو فاتت بالعتق بطلت اليمين تنجيزه الثنتين فيقع والله أعلم .انتهى. وقدمنا أن مما يبطل التعليق لحاقه بدار الحرب قال في المجمع : فلحاقه مرتداً مبطل لتعليقه أي عند الأمام. وقالا لا لأن زوال الملك لا يبطله . وله أن إبقاء تعليقه كفوت محل الجزاء كما إذا قال إن كلمت فلاناً فأنت طالق فمات فلان كذا في النهاية ومنه ما إذا قال إن دخلت هذه الدار فأنت طالق فجعلت الدار بستاناً كما في المعراج وقدمنا أن مما يبطله زوال إمكان البر وذكرنا فروعاً عليه عند شرح قوله وزوال الملك بعد اليمين لا يبطلها». وفي القنية : حلف لا يخرج من بخارى إلا بإذن هؤلاء الثلاثة فجن أحدهم لا يخرج لأنه إن أفاق المجنون حنث ولو مات أحدهم لم يحنث لبطلان اليمين انتهى .
His statement: (And the immediate pronouncement of three invalidates its suspension) i.e., the suspension of three as indicated by most books. The better view is that it refers to the husband to include less than three. I say: the better view is that it refers to divorce because the discourse is about it. So if he said to her: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced three times' or 'once' or 'twice,' then he divorced her three times, then she returned to him after another husband, then she entered, she is not divorced because the consequence is the divorces of this ownership. It is the preventer because the apparent is that nothing new occurs, and the oath is contracted for prevention or constraint. And when the consequence is as we mentioned and it has been lost by the immediate pronouncement of three which invalidates the capacity, then the oath does not remain. It is restricted to three because if he pronounced less than that, the suspension is not invalidated because the consequence remains due to the survival of its place. If he divorced her twice, then she returned to him after another husband, and he had suspended three, then the condition occurred, she is divorced three times unanimously. According to them both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because all the suspended occurs since the second husband demolished the occurring. According to Muhammad, because one of the suspended occurs since the second does not demolish with him. And if the suspended was one divorce and the immediate was two, then she returned to him after another husband, then the condition occurred, then according to Muhammad she becomes prohibited with severe prohibition by the immediate and suspended, but according to them both she is not prohibited since after the occurrence of the suspended two divorces, he owns due to the second demolishing what the first had done. And it is restricted to divorce because if ownership ceases after suspension of emancipation, the suspension is not invalidated, as if he said to his slave: 'If you enter the house, you are free,' then he sold him, then bought him, then he entered, he becomes free because the slave with the quality of being a slave is a place for emancipation, and by sale that quality is not lost. But if it is lost by emancipation, the oath is invalidated, even if he apostatized and joined the abode of war, then was taken captive, then the master owned him and he entered the house, he is not freed. In al-Mi'raj, the correct is 'even if she apostatized' because the apostate is not owned by captivity, it is only in the female slave. And it is restricted to suspension of divorce because immediate pronouncement of three does not invalidate zihar, whether immediate or suspended, as when he said: 'If you enter the house, you are like my mother's back,' then he divorced her three times, then she entered after returning to him after another husband, he is a muzahir because zihar prohibits the act, not the original permissibility. But the existence of marriage is a condition for it, so its continuance is not required for the continuance of the conditioned, like witnesses in marriage. Unlike divorce because it prohibits the original permissibility. The commentator: it is based on consideration of the reality of the condition, as appears from reviewing his words. His statement: (because it is connecting the noun to the verb) has consideration, so reflect. Then: His statement: (I say the better view is that it refers to divorce. He said in al-Nahr: It is not hidden that adding the infinitive to its agent...) And in Fath al-Qadir: Some of our virtuous associates argued that it must not occur except one, like Zufr's saying because they say: the suspended are the divorces of this ownership, and the assumption is that what remains of this ownership is only one, so it becomes like the case where he divorces his wife twice, then says: 'you are divorced three times,' only one occurs because nothing else remains in his possession. The answer: this is conditional, and the meaning is that the suspended are the three divorces of this ownership as long as she exists. When it ceases, the suspended three remain as per the wording, but with the condition of her remaining a place for divorce. So when he pronounces two immediately, the ownership of three ceases, so the suspended three remain as long as her capacity remains and their occurrence is possible. This is established in the immediate pronouncement of two, so they occur. And Allah knows best. End. And we have mentioned that among what invalidates the suspension is his joining the abode of war as an apostate. He said in al-Majma': His joining as an apostate invalidates his suspension according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: No, because cessation of ownership does not invalidate it. His proof: The preservation of his suspension is based on the existence of his eligibility, and by apostasy the protection is lifted, so his suspension does not remain due to loss of eligibility. So if he returns to Islam, that suspension which was ruled to be dropped does not return because the dropped cannot be restored. Similarly in the commentary of the author. And among what invalidates it is the loss of the place of the condition like the loss of the place of the consequence, as when he said: 'If you speak to so-and-so, you are divorced,' then so-and-so dies. And from it, if he said: 'If you enter this house, you are divorced,' then the house was made into a garden. And we have mentioned that among what invalidates it is the cessation of possibility of fulfillment, and we mentioned branches upon it in the explanation of his statement: 'Cessation of ownership after the oath does not invalidate it.' And in al-Quniyah: He swore not to leave Bukhara except with the permission of these three. One of them became insane; he does not leave because if the insane person recovers, he would break his oath. If one of them died, he does not break his oath due to the invalidation of the oath. End.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling that immediate pronouncement (tanziz) of three divorces before the condition invalidates a suspended conditional divorce (ta'liq). The commentator explains that the ta'liq is invalidated because the subject matter (the capacity for divorce in that marriage) is lost when three divorces are pronounced beforehand. The key distinction is that only tanziz of three divorces invalidates ta'liq; tanziz of one or two does not, because the capacity for divorce remains partially. An intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: if the husband suspended three divorces but only pronounced two before the condition, then after a second marriage the condition occurs, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Sahibayn) hold that all three suspended divorces occur (since the second marriage demolishes the previous triple talaq, making the wife again eligible for three), while Muhammad alone holds that only one occurs because he does not accept that the second husband's consummation demolishes the previous divorces completely. Similarly, if the suspended was one and the immediate was two, there is a dispute about the severity of prohibition. The passage also distinguishes divorce from emancipation and zihar: tanziz of three does not invalidate ta'liq of emancipation because the slave remains a valid subject for emancipation even after sale; but it does invalidate ta'liq of divorce because divorce affects the original permissibility of the marriage. Further, apostasy ending the marriage invalidates ta'liq according to Abu Hanifa but not according to the Sahibayn. Various other invalidators are listed: loss of the condition's object (e.g., the person dies) or change of the condition's subject (e.g., house turned into garden). The passage concludes with a ruling from al-Quniyah about an oath dependent on permission and the effect of insanity or death.

Key Terms
تنجيز (tanziz): Immediate pronouncement of divorce without condition.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of divorce on a future condition.
العقر (al-'aqr): Mahr al-mithl (dower) due to a woman who is divorced or freed after conditional intercourse without actual coitus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced three times' and then before she enters, he immediately pronounces two divorces, what is the effect on the suspended divorce?
A) The suspended divorce is invalidated.
B) The suspended divorce remains valid, and if she enters after a second marriage, she is divorced three times according to the Sahibayn.
C) The suspended divorce is cancelled, and she is not divorced even if she enters.
D) The suspended divorce becomes one divorce.

Key Takeaway
The immediate pronouncement of three divorces invalidates a conditional suspension of divorce; this is a specific ruling of the Hanafi school based on the loss of the subject matter.

No Mahr for Staying After Conditional Intercourse
Ruling on absent dower after conditional stay

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that staying (lubth) after a conditional intercourse does not obligate mahr ('aqr) and apply the qiyas analogy to the oath of entry.

Core Idea
Staying is not intercourse; a condition tied to intercourse is not fulfilled by mere staying.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that if a husband suspends triple divorce or emancipation on the occurrence of intercourse (jima'), the wife does not become entitled to the customary dower ('aqr) simply by staying (lubth) without actual intercourse. The author uses a crisp analogy and discusses the linguistic scope of 'jima'' to clarify the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional clause
The scenario: a husband says 'If I have intercourse with you, you are divorced three times' or 'you are free'. The condition is actual jima'.
2
Determine what constitutes jima'
Jima' legally means insertion of the penis into the vagina. It is an instantaneous act without duration.
The passage also notes a broader meaning: agreement or assistance.
3
Analyze the consequence of lubth
Lubth (staying) does not constitute jima', so the condition is not triggered. Therefore, no divorce, emancipation, or mahr results.
4
Compare with the oath analogy
The author draws an analogy: a person who swears not to enter a house while already inside does not break the oath by staying. Similarly, staying does not fulfill a condition of intercourse.
This qiyas shows that the ruling is consistent with general legal principles.
5
Confirm the negated obligation
Since the condition is not met, no 'aqr (mahr al-mithl) is due. The passage ends by affirming this negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو علق الثلاث أو العتق بالوطء لم يجب العقر باللبث) أي لم يجب مهر المثل للمطلقة ثلاثاً والمعتقة بالمكث من غير فعل لأن الجماع هو إدخال الفرج في الفرج وليس له دوام حتى يكون لدوامه حكم ابتدائه كمن حلف لا يدخل هذه الدار وهو فيها لا يحنث باللبث، وكذا لو حلف أن لا يدخل دابته الاصطبل وهي فيه فأمسكها فيه لم يحنث. وفي الفوائد الظهيرية : الجماع عبارة عن الموافقة والمساعدة في أي شيء كان فإن محمداً كثيراً ما يقول في كتاب الحج على أهل المدينة ألستم جامعتمونا في كذا أي وافقتمونا. وحكي عن الطحاوي أنه كان يملي على ابنته مسائل يقول في املائه ألسنا قد جامعناكم على كذا أو لستم قد جامعتمونا على كذا فتبسمت ابنته يوماً من ذلك فوقع بصره عليها فقال: ما شأنك؟ فتبسمت مرة أخرى فأحس الطحاوي أنها ذهبت إلى الجماع المعروف بهذا اللفظ فقال : أو يفهم من هذا؟ فاحترق غضباً وقطع الاملاء ورفع يديه إلى السماء وقال: اللهم لا أريد حياة بعد هذا فتمنى الموت فمات بعد ذلك من نحو خمسة أيام؛ كذا في المعراج. أشار بنفي العقر
His statement: (And if he suspends three or emancipation on intercourse, the 'aqr is not obligated by staying) i.e., the mahr al-mithl is not obligated for the woman divorced three times or the freed woman by staying without action, because copulation is insertion of the penis into the vagina, and it has no duration such that its duration has the ruling of its initiation. Like one who swears not to enter this house while he is in it; he does not break his oath by staying. Similarly, if he swears not to put his animal into the stable while it is in it, and he holds it there, he does not break his oath. And in al-Fawa'id al-Zuhayriyyah: Copulation is an expression of agreement and assistance in anything. Indeed, Muhammad often says in the book of Hajj to the people of Madinah: 'Did you not agree with us in such and such?' i.e., you agreed with us. And it is narrated from al-Tahawi that he was dictating to his daughter, saying in his dictation: 'Have we not conferred with you on such and such? Or have you not conferred with us on such and such?' His daughter smiled one day, so his sight fell upon her. He said: 'What is the matter with you?' She smiled again. Then al-Tahawi realized that she had interpreted it as the well-known copulation with this wording. So he said: 'Is this understood from this?' He burned with anger, stopped dictation, raised his hands to the sky and said: 'O Allah, I do not want life after this.' So he wished for death, and he died after about five days. So in al-Mi'raj. He pointed to the negation of 'aqr.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: when a husband suspends three divorces or emancipation on intercourse (jima'), the wife does not become entitled to mahr al-mithl ('aqr) merely by staying (lubth) without actual intercourse. The reason is that jima' linguistically and legally means insertion of the penis into the vagina (or in legal terms, agreement and assistance). It is an instantaneous act with no duration; therefore, staying does not constitute jima'. The commentator draws an analogy: a person who swears not to enter a house but is already inside does not break the oath by staying; similarly, a person who swears not to put his animal in the stable while it is already there does not break the oath by keeping it there. The passage also notes that the Hanafi jurist Muhammad al-Shaybani uses the term 'jima'' metaphorically for agreement. An anecdote about Imam al-Tahawi illustrates that the literal meaning of jima' can cause confusion; when dictating to his daughter and using 'jima'' in the sense of agreement, she smiled, thinking of coitus. This angered al-Tahawi, leading him to stop dictation and soon after die. The passage ends by affirming that no 'aqr is due.

Key Terms
الوطء (al-wat'): Intercourse; in this context, the condition on which divorce or emancipation is suspended.
العقر (al-'aqr): The mahr al-mithl (customary dower) due to a woman who is divorced or freed after conditional intercourse.
اللبث (al-lubth): Staying, remaining in a state without action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife: 'If I have intercourse with you, you are divorced three times,' and then he stays with her without any sexual act, what is the ruling regarding the dower ('aqr)?
A) She is entitled to 'aqr because staying is a form of cohabitation.
B) She is entitled to 'aqr because the condition is partially fulfilled.
C) She is not entitled to 'aqr because the condition of actual intercourse was not met.
D) She is entitled to 'aqr only if she demands it.

Key Takeaway
Staying after a conditional intercourse does not fulfill the condition; no divorce, emancipation, or mahr results.

Withholding After Intercourse in Conditional Divorce
A commentary expanding on the ruling of continuation after intercourse in conditional divorce, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and links to fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on continuation after intercourse in a conditional divorce oath and explain the intra-Hanafi difference between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Continuation after intercourse without reinsertion does not trigger divorce unless movement occurs; withdrawal is wajib immediately.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a subtle point in conditional divorce: what happens if a husband has intercourse after making a conditional oath but delays withdrawal? The ruling depends on whether he moves or remains still, and there is a difference between the two main disciples of Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and issue
A husband says 'If I have intercourse with you, you are divorced.' He then has intercourse but does not withdraw immediately. The question: does continuation trigger divorce or make him raj'i?
2
Distinguish reinsertion vs. continuation
Muhammad: only actual reinsertion of the penis after withdrawal makes him raj'i. Mere continuation without movement does not. Abu Yusuf: continuation itself is a form of lustful contact that suffices for raj'i.
The key factor is movement.
3
Analyze analogy to fasting
The passage links to Ramadan: if one forgets and then remembers, he must withdraw immediately; if he moves, he must make up and expiate. This reinforces that movement is the decisive element.
4
Understand lexical clarification of 'wat''
The text corrects a mistaken transmission from Ibn Qudamah, affirming that 'wat'' in oaths means actual intercourse, not treading with the foot. The husband's intent does not change the default meaning.
This affects the interpretation of conditional oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب التعليق
مراجعاً في الرجعي إلا إذا أولج ثانياً ولا تطلق في إن نكحتها عليك فهي طالق فنكح
هـ.
اللغة
فقط إلى ثبوت الحرمة باللبث فإن الواجب عليه النزع للحال وإلى أنه لو جامع في رمضان ناسياً فتذكر ودام على ذلك حتى أنزل فعليه القضاء، وإن نزع من ساعته لا. وقيدنا المكث بكونه من غير فعل لأنه لو تحرك لزمه مهربه لأنه كالإيلاج ولذا قالوا أولج ثم قال لها إن جامعتك فأنت طالق أو حرة إن نزع أو لم ينزع ولم يتحرك حتى أنزل لا تطلق ولا تعتق، وإن حرك نفسه طلقت وعتقت ويصير مراجعاً بالحركة الثانية ويجب للأمة العقر ولا حد عليهما. ولو جامع عامداً قبل الفجر وطلع الفجر وجب النزع في الحال فإن حرك نفسه قضى وكفر كما لو حرك بعد التذكر في الأولى؛ كذا في البززية وغيرها من الصوم، وفي المعراج : ولو قال إن وطئتك فيمينه على الجماع. وقال ابن قدامه الحنبلي: وعن محمد بن الحسن يمينه على الوطء بالقدم ولو قال أردت به الجماع ولم يقبل وقد غلط ابن قدامة في النقل عن محمد فإن محمداً ذكر في أيمان الجامع لو قال لها إن وطئتك فهو على الجماع في فرجها بذكره، ولو نوى الدوس بالقدم لا يصدق في الصرف عن الجماع ويحنث بالدوس بالقدم أيضاً لاعترافه به على نفسه. ولو قال إن وطئت من غير ذكر امرأة فهو على الدوس بالقدم وهو في والعرف باتفاق أصحابنا ا والعقر بالضم مهر المرأة إذا وطئت على شبهة، وبالفتح الجرح من عقره أي جرحه فهو عقير ؛ كذا في الصحاح وفي القاموس : العُقر بالضم دية الفرج المغصوب وصداق المرأة ا هـ . وفي المصباح : العُقر بالضم دية فرج المرأة إذا غصبت على نفسها ثم كثر ذلك حتى استعمل في المهر انتهى . واللبث من لبث بالمكان لبثا من باب تعب، وجاء في المصدر السكون للتخفيف واللبث بالفتح والمرة بالكسر الهيئة، والنوع والاسم اللبث بالضم كذا في المصباح. وفي القاموس : اللبث بفتح اللام وسكون الباء المكث من لبث كسمع وهو نادر لأن المصدر من فعل بالكسر قياسه التحريك إذا لم يتعد انتهى. وهو أولى مما في المصباح لإيهامه أن المصدر بفتح الباء وأن السكون جائز
Book of Divorce, Chapter on Suspensive Clauses. ... He becomes a revocable divorcer only if he reinserts a second time, and she is not divorced if he says 'If I marry a woman upon you, she is divorced' and then marries ... language. ... Only to the establishment of prohibition by staying; for it is obligatory upon him to withdraw immediately. And that if he had intercourse in Ramadan forgetfully, then remembered and continued until he ejaculated, then he must make up; if he withdrew immediately, no. And we restricted 'staying' to being without movement, because if he moved, the escape becomes necessary for him because it is like insertion. That is why they said: if he inserted, then said to her, 'If I have intercourse with you, you are divorced' or 'free', whether he withdrew or did not withdraw, and did not move until he ejaculated, she is not divorced and not free. But if he moved himself, she is divorced and freed, and he becomes a revocable divorcer by the second movement, and the slave woman is owed the 'aqr, and there is no hadd on both. And if he had intercourse deliberately before dawn and dawn came, it is obligatory to withdraw immediately. If he moved himself, he must make up and expiate, just as if he moved after remembering in the first case. Thus in al-Bazzaziyya and other works on fasting. And in al-Mi'raj: if he said 'If I tread upon you', his oath is about intercourse. Ibn Qudamah al-Hanbali said: from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, his oath is about treading with the foot; even if he said 'I meant intercourse', it is not accepted. And Ibn Qudamah made a mistake in transmission from Muhammad; for Muhammad stated in Ayman al-Jami': if he said to her 'If I tread upon you' it is about intercourse in her vagina with his penis. And if he intended treading with the foot, he is not believed in turning it away from intercourse, and he incurs the penalty by treading with the foot also because of his admission against himself. And if he said 'If I tread' without mentioning a woman, it is about treading with the foot, and that is according to 'urf by agreement of our companions. And 'aqr with dammah: the mahr of a woman if she is penetrated under uncertainty; with fathah: the wound, from 'aqaraha meaning he wounded her, so he is 'aqir. Thus in al-Sihah. In al-Qamus: al-'uqr with dammah is the compensation for the violated vagina and the dower of the woman. In al-Misbah: al-'uqr with dammah is the compensation for a woman's vagina if she is violated, then it became frequent until used for mahr. And al-labth: from labitha fi al-makan labthan from the category of ta'iba; the verbal noun (masdar) can have sukun for ease; al-labth with fathah, and the single instance with kasrah is the mode and type, and the noun is al-lubth with dammah. In al-Qamus: al-labth with fathah of lam and sukun of ba is al-makth from labith like sami'a, and it is rare because the masdar from fi'l with kasrah normally has vowel movement if it is intransitive. And this is more correct than what is in al-Misbah because it gives the impression that the masdar has fathah of ba and that sukun is permissible.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the chapter of suspensive clauses in divorce. It begins by clarifying a prior ruling: in conditional divorce, merely continuing intercourse after an oath does not automatically trigger the divorce or make the husband a revocable divorcer (raj'i) unless he actually reinserts. The primary issue is about a husband who says 'If I have intercourse with you, you are divorced' and then has intercourse but delays withdrawal. According to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, he does not become raj'i because the continuation is not considered a new act of sexual engagement. Abu Yusuf, however, considers it raj'i due to the presence of lustful contact, which is the stronger analogy. The author suggests that Abu Yusuf's view should be relied upon. The text then links this to fasting: if one deliberately continues intercourse after remembering, he must both make up and expiate. The discussion extends to the interpretation of 'wat' (treading/coitus) in oaths. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan insists that 'wat' means actual intercourse in the vagina, not treading with the foot. Ibn Qudamah's contrary transmission from Muhammad is rejected as erroneous. The passage ends with lexical clarifications: 'aqr (mahr for doubtful intercourse) and 'labth' (staying, with variant vowel patterns). The author prefers the Qamus's definition of labth as the standard masdar with sukun as rare.

Key Terms
اللبث (al-labth): Staying in place without movement; in this context, delaying withdrawal after intercourse.
العقر (al-'aqr): Compensation for intercourse under uncertainty; dower.
وطء (wat'): Sexual intercourse in the vagina with the penis, not treading with the foot.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband says 'If I have intercourse with you, you are divorced' and then has intercourse but delays withdrawal without moving, what is the ruling?
A) She is divorced and he becomes raj'i.
B) She is not divorced and he does not become raj'i.
C) She is divorced but he does not become raj'i.
D) He must expiate for delaying.

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce, continuation after intercourse without movement or reinsertion does not trigger the divorce; the husband must actively withdraw, and movement constitutes a new act that does trigger it.

Marriage During Iddah After Bain Divorce: The Condition of 'Upon You'
Ruling on conditional divorce when husband divorces wife bain and marries another in her iddah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a new wife is not divorced when a husband marries her during the iddah of a bain divorce after a conditional oath involving the phrase 'upon you', and apply the underlying principle to similar conditional divorce scenarios.

Core Idea
A condition requiring marital rivalry is not triggered when the first wife is bain—she is no longer a wife.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq concerning a conditional divorce. A husband swears that if he marries a woman 'upon you', she is divorced. He then divorces his wife bain (irrevocably) and, during her iddah, marries another. The key question is whether the new wife is divorced by the condition. The Hanafi ruling hinges on the precise meaning of 'upon you'—which implies marital rivalry—and its absence when the first wife is no longer a wife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The husband's conditional statement and subsequent marriage during iddah. The ruling: the new wife is not divorced.
2
State the Hanafi position
The condition is not fulfilled because 'upon you' implies contention in bed and division, which does not exist when the first wife is bain.
3
Locate the dalil
The proof is the literal meaning of the condition. The text says: 'because marrying upon her means entering upon her one who contends with her in bed and competes with her in division, and it has not occurred.'
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'عليك' (upon you) is interpreted as entering a state of rivalry. Only a current wife can have such rivalry. Since the bain divorce severs marital ties, there is no rivalry at the time of the new marriage.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars may interpret the condition literally without requiring rivalry. The text contrasts with raj'i divorce, where the wife remains a wife, so the condition would be triggered.
This highlights the practical difference between bain and raj'i divorces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا) تطلق في أن نكحتها عليك فهي طالق فنكح عليها في عدة البائن) يعني تطلق امرأته الجديدة فيما . قال للتي تحته إن تزوجت عليك امرأة فهي طالق فطلق امرأته بائناً ثم تزوّج أخرى في عدتها لأن الشرط لم يوجد لأن التزوّج عليها أن يدخل عليها من ينازعها في الفراش ويزاحمها في القسم ولم يوجد قيد بالبائن لأنه لو كان رجعياً طلقت كما في شرح مسكين. وفي البزازية من فصل الأمر باليد جعل أمر المرأة التي يتزوجها عليها بأن قال إن تزوجت عليك امرأة فأمرها بيدك أو قال ما دمت امرأتي ثم طلقها بائناً أو خالعها وتزوج أخرى في عدتها ثم تزوّج بالأولى لا يصير الأمر بيدها لأن المراد حال المنازعة في القسم ولم يوجد وقت الإدخال وإن قال إن تزوّجت امرأة فأمرها بيدك فأبانها ثم تزوّج بأخرى صار الأمر بيدها .ا.هـ. وفي القنية من باب تفويض الطلاق: إن تزوجت عليك امرأة فأمرها بيدك ثم دخلت المرأة في نكاحه بنكاح الفضولي وأجاز بالفعل ليس لها أن تطلقها، ولو قال إن دخلت امرأة في نكاحي فلها ذلك وكذا في التوكيل بذلك انتهى. وفي آخر الأيمان : إن سكنت في هذه البلدة فامرأته طالق وخرج في الفور وخلع امرأته ثم سكنها قبل انقضاء عدتها لا تطلق لأنها ليست بامرأته وقت وجود الشرط. قال إن فعلت كذا فحلال الله علي حرام ثم قال إن فعلت كذا فحلال الله علي حرام لفعل الآخر ففعل أحد الفعلين حتى بانت امرأته ثم فعل الآخر فقيل لا يقع الثاني لأنها ليست بامرأته عند الشرط، وقيل يقع وهو
His saying: 'And she is not divorced in the case: If I marry a woman upon you, she is divorced, then he marries upon her in the 'iddah of the final divorce' means: his new wife is not divorced in the matter. He said to his under-wife: If I marry a woman upon you, she is divorced. Then he divorced his wife finally, then married another in her 'iddah, because the condition has not occurred: because 'marrying upon her' means entering upon her one who contends with her in bed and competes with her in division, and it has not occurred. He restricted it to final divorce because if it had been revocable, she would be divorced, as in Sharh Miskin. In al-Bazzaziyya, from the chapter on delegation of the right of divorce: he made the matter of the woman he marries upon her in her hand by saying: If I marry a woman upon you, her matter is in your hand, or he said: as long as you are my wife, then he divorced her finally or khala'a her, and married another in her 'iddah, then married the first, the matter does not come into her hand because what is intended is the state of contention in division, and it did not exist at the time of entering. And if he said: If I marry a woman, her matter is in your hand, then he divorced her finally, then married another, her matter came into her hand. In al-Qunyah, from the chapter of delegation of divorce: If you marry a woman upon you, her matter is in your hand, then a woman entered into his marriage through the marriage of an unauthorized agent and he ratified it by action, she does not have the right to divorce her. And if he said: If a woman enters into my marriage, she has that — and similarly in giving agency. In the last part on oaths: If I reside in this town, his wife is divorced, and he left immediately and divorced his wife, then resided there before the expiration of her 'iddah, she is not divorced because she is not his wife at the time the condition is fulfilled. He said: If I do such and such, then Allah's lawful is forbidden to me, then he said: If I do such and such, then Allah's lawful is forbidden to me, for the other action, so he did one of the two actions until his wife became finally divorced, then did the other. Some say the second does not occur because she is not his wife at the condition, and some say it does occur, and it is...
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific conditional divorce scenario. The husband says to his current wife: 'If I marry a woman upon you, she is divorced.' Then he divorces this wife with a final (bain) divorce. During her 'iddah (waiting period), he marries another woman. The question is whether the new wife becomes divorced by the original condition. The ruling is that she does not, because the phrase 'upon you' implies rivalry and contention in the marriage bed and division of nights. During the 'iddah of a bain divorce, the first wife is no longer his wife, so there is no rivalry. Had the divorce been revocable (raj'i), she would still be his wife and the condition would have been fulfilled, so the new wife would be divorced. The text also discusses related cases from al-Bazzaziyya and al-Qunyah, including cases where the husband delegates the power of divorce to the wife in the conditional phrase. For example, if he says 'If I marry a woman upon you, her matter is in your hand' and then divorces her finally, then marries another during iddah, the first wife's power does not return because the condition for contention is absent. However, if he divorces her first and then marries another without the 'upon you' phrase, the power is granted. The passage ends with a general principle: if a condition is attached to a marital relationship, it only applies while that relationship exists. The text is cut off but the main ruling is clear.

Key Terms
عدة البائن ('iddat al-ba'in): The waiting period after a final (irrevocable) divorce; during this time the woman is not a wife for purposes of marital rights.
الزوجة عليها (al-zawjah 'alayha): Marrying a woman 'upon' another woman means he brings in a rival who contends for bed and division.
البائن (al-ba'in): Irrevocable divorce that severs the marital tie immediately; the wife goes into iddah without spousal rights.
الرجعي (al-raj'i): Revocable divorce; the wife remains in marriage during iddah and can be taken back without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife: 'If I marry a woman upon you, she is divorced.' He then divorces her with a bain divorce and marries another woman during her iddah. Is the new wife divorced?
A) Yes, because the condition is fulfilled by the marriage contract alone.
B) No, because the first wife is no longer his wife, so there is no rivalry.
C) Yes, because the condition mentions 'upon you' which still applies during iddah.
D) No, because the husband did not intend the new wife to be divorced.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce emphasizes that conditions must be interpreted contextually; the phrase 'upon you' implies marital rivalry, which is absent after a bain divorce.

Conditional Divorce and the Master's Oath: Key Rulings
Ruling statement with applied cases and a legal trick.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce when the wife is irrevocably divorced and apply the legal trick for a master's oath.

Core Idea
A condition requiring a co-wife is not fulfilled if the first wife is irrevocably divorced at the time of the new marriage.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings on conditional divorces and oaths. The central question is what happens when a man conditions a divorce on an event that occurs after his wife has become irrevocably divorced. The passage also provides a legitimate legal stratagem (hilah) for a master who swears an oath concerning his slave-girl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a conditional divorce: a man says to his wife 'If I marry another woman upon you, she is divorced.' Then he divorces his wife irrevocably and marries another during her iddah. The ruling is that the new wife is not divorced.
The condition 'marrying upon you' means bringing in a co-wife who contests in bed and division.
2
Distinguish between ba'in and raj'i
If the first divorce is revocable (raj'i), the wife remains a wife during iddah, so the condition is fulfilled and the new wife is divorced. If irrevocable (ba'in), she is no longer a wife, so the condition is not met.
This distinction is crucial for the thamarah al-khilaf.
3
Analyze the delegation case
If the husband says 'If I marry a woman upon you, her command is in your hand,' then divorces irrevocably and marries another, the ex-wife does not get the power of command. But if he says generally 'If I marry a woman,' she does get it.
The wording matters: 'upon you' implies contestation, which is absent after ba'in.
4
Examine the hilah for the master
A master swears to his slave-girl: 'If I have intercourse with you, you are free.' The trick is to sell her, marry her as a free woman, have intercourse (dissolving the oath), then buy her back and have intercourse again without her becoming free.
This hilah is permissible according to Hanafis; it avoids the consequence without lying.
5
Review other oath rulings
The passage includes rulings on oaths about sexual relations. For example, if a man swears not to approach his wife and she fulfills her need from him while he lies down, he breaks the oath. But if he is asleep, he does not. Also, an oath about 'covering' is about withdrawal then insertion.
These detailed rulings show the precision of Hanafi fiqh in oath-taking.

Source Text

إذا أخرج ثم أولج في العتق ينبغي أن يجب الحد لأنه وطء لا في ملك ولا في شبهة العدة بخلاف الطلاق لوجود العدة، وجوابه ما ذكر في الكتاب أن هذا ليس بابتداء فعل من كل وجه لاتحاد المجلس والمقصود ا هـ . وقيدنا Book of Divorce, Chapter of Conditional Divorce. She is not divorced when marrying during the iddah of an irrevocable divorce, nor in [the case of] 'you are divorced if God wills' with connection, even if she dies before his saying 'if God wills'. And in al-Mi'raj: the mahr is obligatory because the respected vulva is not devoid of a dower or a substitute. And in al-Mi'raj: one may say that if he withdraws then inserts in the case of emancipation, the hadd penalty should be obligatory because it is intercourse not in ownership nor in a semblance of iddah, unlike divorce due to the existence of iddah. Its answer is what is mentioned in the book that this is not an initiating act from every aspect due to the unity of the session and the objective. We restricted it to conditional to avoid what was narrated from Muhammad: if a man committed adultery with a woman then married her in that state, then if he persisted on that and did not withdraw, two mahr are obligatory: mahr for intercourse and mahr for the contract, even if he did not reinsert because his continuance on that is above seclusion after the contract. Thus they transmitted, and the specification of the narration to Muhammad does not indicate disagreement but because it was narrated from him alone. In al-Bazzaziyyah: He swore not to approach her, so he lay down and she came and fulfilled her need from him — he breaks his oath according to the fatwa. If he was asleep, he does not break it. He said to his slave-girl: if I have intercourse with you, you are free. The trick is that he sells her to someone else, then marries her and has intercourse with her, so the oath is dissolved without consequence, then he buys her back from him and has intercourse with her, so she does not become free. He swore not to cover her while he was upon her — the oath is about withdrawal then insertion; if he continues upon her, he does not break it. And it was mentioned at the beginning of the thirteenth chapter on intercourse: he does not break his oath with intercourse below the vulva even if he ejaculates, unless he intended. End. His saying: (And she is not divorced in 'if I marry you upon you, she is divorced' so he married upon her during the iddah of an irrevocable divorce) — meaning his new wife is not divorced. He said to the wife under him: if I marry a woman upon you, she is divorced, then he divorces his wife irrevocably, then marries another during her iddah, because the condition did not occur. Because 'marrying upon her' means bringing in one who contests her in bed and vies with her in division, and it did not occur. He restricted it to irrevocable because if it were revocable, she would be divorced, as in Sharh Miskin. In al-Bazzaziyyah, from the section 'command in hand': He placed the command of the woman he marries upon her by saying 'if I marry a woman upon you, her command is in your hand' or saying 'as long as you are my wife', then he divorces her irrevocably or khul's her, and marries another during her iddah, then marries the first — the command does not become in her hand because what is meant is the situation of contest in division, and it did not exist at the time of entry. But if he said 'if I marry a woman, her command is in your hand', then he irrevocably divorces her, then marries another, the command becomes in her hand. End. In al-Qunyah, from the chapter of delegating divorce: If he says 'if I marry a woman upon you, her command is in your hand', then the woman enters his marriage through a marriage by a guardian without permission and he approves it by action, she does not have the right to divorce her. If he says 'if a woman enters my marriage, she has that', likewise in delegation. End. In the last part of oaths: He said 'if I reside in this town, my wife is divorced', and he immediately left and divorced his wife, then resided in it before the expiry of her iddah — she is not divorced because she was not his wife at the time of the condition. He said 'if I do such-and-such, God's lawful is forbidden to me', then said 'if I do such-and-such, God's lawful is forbidden to me' for the other action, then he did one of the two actions until his wife became irrevocably divorced, then did the other — it was said the second does not occur because she is not his wife at the condition; and it was said it does occur, and that is his saying: (Because his continuance on that is above seclusion after the contract). He said in al-Nahr: This is problematic based on what passed, for he assigned to this single act a separate ruling. End. And some answered that what passed is based on what is more apparent. End. In al-Qunyah: He divorced her then said 'if I hold my wife until my death, she is divorced three', he leaves her until her iddah expires, then marries her after a day — it does not occur because with the passing of the iddah she exited from being his wife, so by marriage he did not hold his wife. End.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses several rulings under the chapter of conditional divorce (ta'liq). The central ruling is that if a man says to his wife 'If I marry another woman upon you, she is divorced,' and then divorces his wife irrevocably (ba'in) and marries another woman during her iddah period, the new wife is not divorced. The reason is that the condition 'marrying upon you' means bringing in a co-wife who will contest with her in bed and in division of nights. Since the first wife is no longer his wife (due to irrevocable divorce) at the time of the new marriage, she is not in a position to be a co-wife, so the condition is not fulfilled. If the divorce were revocable (raj'i), the wife remains his wife during iddah and the condition would be met. The passage also includes a legal trick (hilah) for a master who swore to his slave-girl that if he had intercourse with her she would be free: he can sell her to another, then marry her (as a free woman) and have intercourse, thereby dissolving the oath without penalty, then buy her back. Other cases cover oaths about sexual relations, where the ruling depends on specific actions. The passage concludes with a disagreement about whether a second conditional divorce occurs after the wife has become irrevocably divorced: some say it does not because she is not a wife at the condition, while others say it does due to continuance of the act being akin to seclusion after contract. The commentary in al-Nahr finds this latter opinion problematic, but an answer is given.

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; a divorce that ends the marriage immediately without the possibility of revocation during iddah.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying.
حيلة (hilah): A legally permissible stratagem to avoid a negative consequence, such as an oath or obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, if a man says 'If I marry another woman upon you, she is divorced' and then divorces his wife irrevocably (ba'in), what is the status of the new wife he marries during the iddah?
A) She is automatically divorced.
B) She is not divorced because the first wife is irrevocably divorced.
C) She is divorced only if the first wife was pregnant.
D) She is divorced if the husband intended it.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce based on 'marrying upon' the wife is not triggered if the wife is irrevocably divorced at the time of the new marriage.

Dispute on Multiple Conditional Divorces After Irrevocable Divorce
Intra-Hanafi dispute with critical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi opinions on whether a second conditional divorce occurs after the wife has become irrevocably divorced and evaluate the tarjih.

Core Idea
If a wife is irrevocably divorced, a subsequent conditional divorce does not take effect because she is no longer a wife.

This lesson examines a dispute among Hanafi scholars about multiple conditional oaths. If a person makes two conditional statements, and the wife becomes irrevocably divorced after the first condition is fulfilled, does the second condition trigger another divorce? The passage presents two views and an analysis from later commentators.

Guided Path
1
State the two opinions
Opinion 1: The second divorce does not occur because the wife is not a wife at the time of the condition. Opinion 2: It does occur because continuance of the act is above seclusion after contract.
The passage says 'فقيل لا يقع الثاني' and 'وقيل يقع'.
2
Identify the dalil for each
Opinion 1: The condition requires a wife in existence. Opinion 2: The act is treated as a single continuous act, so the condition is considered to have occurred during marriage.
The second opinion relies on the concept of 'دوامه على ذلك فوق الخلوة بعد العقد'.
3
Analyze the objection from al-Nahr
Al-Nahr criticizes the second opinion, saying it assigns a separate ruling to a single act, which is problematic.
The objection is that the same act cannot have two different rulings depending on timing.
4
Understand the answer
Some scholars answer that the earlier ruling (the first condition) is based on what is more apparent (al-adhar), implying the later ruling (second condition) follows the dominant view.
The answer suggests that the second opinion is weaker and the relied-upon view is that the second divorce does not occur.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The consensus among later Hanafi jurists is that the second conditional divorce does not occur after irrevocable divorce. This is the operating position for fatwa.
The text inclines to this view, as it says 'ما مر مبني على ما هو الأظهر'.

Source Text

كتاب الطلاق باب التعليق. عليها في عدة البائن ولا في أنت طالق إن شاء الله متصلاً وإن ماتت قبل قوله إن وهي لا في المعراج ووجب المهر لأن البضع المحترم لا يخلو عن عقر أو عقر. وفي المعراج : ولقائل أن يقول إذا أخرج ثم أولج في العتق ينبغي أن يجب الحد لأنه وطء لا في ملك ولا في شبهة العدة بخلاف الطلاق لوجود العدة، وجوابه ما ذكر في الكتاب أن هذا ليس بابتداء فعل من كل وجه لاتحاد المجلس والمقصود ا هـ . وقيدنا بالتعليق للاحتراز عما روي عن محمد لو أن رجلاً زنى بامرأة ثم تزوجها في تلك الحالة فإن لبث على ذلك ولم ينزع وجب مهران مهر بالوطء ومهر بالعقد، وإن لم يستأنف الإدخال لأن دوامه على ذلك فوق الخلوة بعد العقد؛ كذا نقلوا وتخصيص الرواية بمحمد لا يدل على خلاف بل لأنها رويت عنه دون غيره. وفي البزازية : حلف لا يقربها فاستلقى وجاءت وقضت منه حاجتها يحنث فيما عليه الفتوى ولو نائماً لا يحنث قال لأمته إن جامعتك فأنت حرة فالحيلة أن يبيعها من غيره ثم يتزوجها ويطؤها فتنحل لا إلى جزاء ثم يشتريها منه فيطؤها فلا تعتق حلف لا يغشاها وهو عليها فاليمين على الإخراج ثم الإدخال فإن دام عليها لا يحنث وذكر في أول الفصل الثالث عشر في الجماع لا يحنث بالجماع فيما دون الفرج وإن أنزل إلا إذا نوى انتهى . قوله: (ولا) تطلق في أن نكحتها عليك فهي طالق فنكح عليها في عدة البائن) يعني تطلق امرأته الجديدة فيما . قال للتي رجعياً طلقت كما في شرح مسكين. وفي البزازية من فصل الأمر باليد جعل أمر المرأة التي يتزوجها عليها بأن قال إن تزوجت عليك امرأة فأمرها بيدك أو قال ما دمت امرأتي ثم طلقها بائناً أو خالعها وتزوج أخرى في عدتها ثم تزوّج بالأولى لا يصير الأمر بيدها لأن المراد حال المنازعة في القسم ولم يوجد وقت الإدخال وإن قال إن تزوّجت امرأة فأمرها بيدك فأبانها ثم تزوّج بأخرى صار الأمر بيدها .ا.هـ. وفي القنية من باب تفويض الطلاق: إن تزوجت عليك امرأة فأمرها بيدك ثم دخلت المرأة في نكاحه بنكاح الفضولي وأجاز بالفعل ليس لها أن تطلقها، ولو قال إن دخلت امرأة في نكاحي فلها ذلك وكذا في التوكيل بذلك انتهى. وفي آخر الأيمان : إن سكنت في هذه البلدة فامرأته طالق وخرج في الفور وخلع امرأته ثم سكنها قبل انقضاء عدتها لا تطلق لأنها ليست بامرأته وقت وجود الشرط. قال إن فعلت كذا فحلال الله علي حرام ثم قال إن فعلت كذا فحلال الله على ما مر إذ قد جعل لآخر هذا الفعل الواحد حكم على حدة اهـ. وأجاب بعضهم بأن ما مر مبني على ما هو الأظهر انتهى. وفي القنية : طلقها ثم قال إن أمسكت امرأتي إلى مماتي فهي طالق ثلاثاً يتركها حتى تنقضي عدتها ثم يتزوجها بعد يوم لا يقع لأنها بمضي العدة خرجت عن أن تكون امرأته فبالنكاح لم يمسك امرأته انتهى
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Conditional Divorce. She is not divorced when marrying during the iddah of an irrevocable divorce, nor in [the case of] 'you are divorced if God wills' with connection, even if she dies before his saying 'if God wills'. And in al-Mi'raj: the mahr is obligatory because the respected vulva is not devoid of a dower or a substitute. And in al-Mi'raj: one may say that if he withdraws then inserts in the case of emancipation, the hadd penalty should be obligatory because it is intercourse not in ownership nor in a semblance of iddah, unlike divorce due to the existence of iddah. Its answer is what is mentioned in the book that this is not an initiating act from every aspect due to the unity of the session and the objective. We restricted it to conditional to avoid what was narrated from Muhammad: if a man committed adultery with a woman then married her in that state, then if he persisted on that and did not withdraw, two mahr are obligatory: mahr for intercourse and mahr for the contract, even if he did not reinsert because his continuance on that is above seclusion after the contract. Thus they transmitted, and the specification of the narration to Muhammad does not indicate disagreement but because it was narrated from him alone. In al-Bazzaziyyah: He swore not to approach her, so he lay down and she came and fulfilled her need from him — he breaks his oath according to the fatwa. If he was asleep, he does not break it. He said to his slave-girl: if I have intercourse with you, you are free. The trick is that he sells her to someone else, then marries her and has intercourse with her, so the oath is dissolved without consequence, then he buys her back from him and has intercourse with her, so she does not become free. He swore not to cover her while he was upon her — the oath is about withdrawal then insertion; if he continues upon her, he does not break it. And it was mentioned at the beginning of the thirteenth chapter on intercourse: he does not break his oath with intercourse below the vulva even if he ejaculates, unless he intended. End. His saying: (And she is not divorced in 'if I marry you upon you, she is divorced' so he married upon her during the iddah of an irrevocable divorce) — meaning his new wife is not divorced. He said to the wife under him: if I marry a woman upon you, she is divorced, then he divorces his wife irrevocably, then marries another during her iddah, because the condition did not occur. Because 'marrying upon her' means bringing in one who contests her in bed and vies with her in division, and it did not occur. He restricted it to irrevocable because if it were revocable, she would be divorced, as in Sharh Miskin. In al-Bazzaziyyah, from the section 'command in hand': He placed the command of the woman he marries upon her by saying 'if I marry a woman upon you, her command is in your hand' or saying 'as long as you are my wife', then he divorces her irrevocably or khul's her, and marries another during her iddah, then marries the first — the command does not become in her hand because what is meant is the situation of contest in division, and it did not exist at the time of entry. But if he said 'if I marry a woman, her command is in your hand', then he irrevocably divorces her, then marries another, the command becomes in her hand. End. In al-Qunyah, from the chapter of delegating divorce: If he says 'if I marry a woman upon you, her command is in your hand', then the woman enters his marriage through a marriage by a guardian without permission and he approves it by action, she does not have the right to divorce her. If he says 'if a woman enters my marriage, she has that', likewise in delegation. End. In the last part of oaths: He said 'if I reside in this town, my wife is divorced', and he immediately left and divorced his wife, then resided in it before the expiry of her iddah — she is not divorced because she was not his wife at the time of the condition. He said 'if I do such-and-such, God's lawful is forbidden to me', then said 'if I do such-and-such, God's lawful is forbidden to me' for the other action, then he did one of the two actions until his wife became irrevocably divorced, then did the other — it was said the second does not occur because she is not his wife at the condition; and it was said it does occur, and that is his saying: (Because his continuance on that is above seclusion after the contract). He said in al-Nahr: This is problematic based on what passed, for he assigned to this single act a separate ruling. End. And some answered that what passed is based on what is more apparent. End. In al-Qunyah: He divorced her then said 'if I hold my wife until my death, she is divorced three', he leaves her until her iddah expires, then marries her after a day — it does not occur because with the passing of the iddah she exited from being his wife, so by marriage he did not hold his wife. End.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses several rulings under the chapter of conditional divorce (ta'liq). The central ruling is that if a man says to his wife 'If I marry another woman upon you, she is divorced,' and then divorces his wife irrevocably (ba'in) and marries another woman during her iddah period, the new wife is not divorced. The reason is that the condition 'marrying upon you' means bringing in a co-wife who will contest with her in bed and in division of nights. Since the first wife is no longer his wife (due to irrevocable divorce) at the time of the new marriage, she is not in a position to be a co-wife, so the condition is not fulfilled. If the divorce were revocable (raj'i), the wife remains his wife during iddah and the condition would be met. The passage also includes a legal trick (hilah) for a master who swore to his slave-girl that if he had intercourse with her she would be free: he can sell her to another, then marry her (as a free woman) and have intercourse, thereby dissolving the oath without penalty, then buy her back. Other cases cover oaths about sexual relations, where the ruling depends on specific actions. The passage concludes with a disagreement about whether a second conditional divorce occurs after the wife has become irrevocably divorced: some say it does not because she is not a wife at the condition, while others say it does due to continuance of the act being akin to seclusion after contract. The commentary in al-Nahr finds this latter opinion problematic, but an answer is given.

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; a divorce that ends the marriage immediately without the possibility of revocation during iddah.
دوام (dawam): Continuance; the state of an action continuing over time. In this context, it is argued that the second act is a continuation of the first.
خلوة بعد العقد (khalwah ba'da al-'aqd): Seclusion after a marriage contract; a legal construct that, under certain conditions, treats seclusion as actual intercourse for rights like mahr and iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi opinion, what happens if a person makes two conditional oaths on his wife's divorce, the first triggers irrevocable divorce, and then he fulfills the second condition?
A) The second divorce occurs because the condition is fulfilled.
B) The second divorce does not occur because the wife is no longer a wife.
C) Both divorces are considered one.
D) The second divorce occurs only if the first divorce was revocable.

Key Takeaway
A second conditional divorce does not take effect after the wife has become irrevocably divorced, because the condition lacks its subject.

Talaq — Divorce

The Husband's Claim of Istithnāʾ and the Intra-Hanafī Dispute
Intra-Hanafī dispute on accepting husband's istithnāʾ claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the acceptance of the husband's claim of exception in divorce and identify the muftā bihi position with its rationale.

Core Idea
In a time of corruption, the husband's claim of exception in divorce is not accepted—precaution overrides the general rule.

You have likely encountered the principle 'al-qawl qawluhu' in family law. In ṭalāq, a husband's claim about his own utterances is generally accepted. But what if he claims he said 'in shāʾ Allāh' after pronouncing divorce? This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafī dispute where the later school chose precaution over principle, and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Start with the default: in claims regarding divorce (including istithnāʾ), the husband's word is accepted (qawl qawluhu) because he is the one who spoke. Note that evidence can be brought to prove he did not make an exception.
This is based on the principle that the husband is the actor, and his statement is presumptively true.
2
Locate the intra-madhhab dispute
The passage cites al-Nawādir, where Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad differ. Abū Yūsuf holds the husband's claim of istithnāʾ is accepted; Muḥammad rejects it and says the divorce occurs immediately.
This dispute is within the Hanafi school, not across madhāhib.
3
Analyze each Imām's dalīl
Abū Yūsuf's proof: the possibility that the husband said it and witnesses did not hear, so his word is taken. Muḥammad's proof: to prevent abuse in a corrupt age, as a precaution for the sanctity of marriage (furooj).
Note the shift from textual principle to public interest.
4
Determine the muftā bihi
The text explicitly states that Muḥammad's view is the relied-upon position (alayhi al-iʿtimād wa al-fatwā) as a precaution in times of corruption.
This is a case where a Nawādir opinion overrides Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah for practical reasons.
5
Apply to a scenario
If a husband says 'You are divorced' and later claims he said 'in shāʾ Allāh', under the muftā bihi view the divorce is effective immediately; his claim is rejected.
The same ruling applies to claims of conditional divorce or khulʿ exceptions.

Source Text
